O, sons, as you will have completed this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, by its fulfillment in it, and by its expositions and by its declarations, to me it seems that you complete that for yourselves with this treatise, which is a part of the operation by hand, and is surgery.
For the operation by hand is spread out in our land and in our times of all deprived, while greatly fearing the science of it, its practices are obstructed; and nothing remains of it except some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which altered the most and led to those errors and misunderstandings among those whose understanding of it was closed and its force and art prolonged.
And it seems to me that you vivify that by the arrangement of this treatise in accordance with the way of exposition and declaration and of abbreviation; and so that it comes through the forms of the tools of cautery and other instruments of the work as though it were a preparation of the declaration, which is necessary.
But the cause for which no good artist is found by hand in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and that its practitioner before anything be exercised in the science of anatomy, which knows the joints of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and have knowledge of the bones, of the nerves and of the ligaments, and the number of those, and their discharge; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and the locations of their exit from them.
And so says Hippocrates that by name is great, yet in work small, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said of that in the introduction to this book; for who is not learned that which we have said of anatomy, it is not vacated that he does not fall into error by which men will die, as I have seen too many of those, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves from it without science or experience.
For I saw a foolish and experienced doctor who pricked an abscess scrofulous on the neck of a woman, and pierced some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed and the woman died in his arms.
And I saw another doctor who before he swallowed a stone from a man who had a process in his state, and the stone was large, and it came to him and drew that stone with a piece of the vesica, so that the man on the third day died.
And I called out to extract that, and saw that by the size of the stone and by the disposition of the patient over him that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who received stipends from a duke of our land for surgery, and indeed gave to a black slave whose leg was broken by a fall; and prepared the doctor with his ignorance, extracted the fracture above the plague, with powders and splints with great tightness, and did not leave the plague to breathe.
And then he released him according to his desires, and then he layed, and then he released it for several days and commanded that we unbind the bandage; and so it stayed as though the leg and thigh had apostematized, and came to perdition.
And I was called to him, and prepared to unbind the bandage, and obtained tranquillity and ceased from his pains.
But indeed corruption was confirmed in the member, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to wander in the member until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who perforated an apostematoid ulcer, and was open for several days, while the wickedness of it magnified with its lord.
The cause of which is that the ulcerative humor is pure of melancholic humor, then it does not come to him by iron from all over, but that there be some member in which he suffers that from all over he is hostile.
And so, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be divided into two divisions: one in operation to him who is associated with health, and in work with which there is peril in the greater dispositions.
And I have already excused that in all places of this book in which it occurs in operation in which there is error and fear; so that it is necessary for you that you guard that and leave that, so that fools do not obstruct the way to speak and to you discredit.
And so take your arms with solicitude and with protection of God, and your patients with gentleness and with firmness.
And use the better way by the grace of God to health and to laudable success, and lay the terrible diseases of difficult cure, and place your arms from that which causes you to fear, so that solicitude in your faith and in your name does not evade you: for it remains to your glory, and more in the world and lastly to your sanctities.
Gaulish already said in some of his maxims: Do not heal bad diseases, so that bad doctors do not be named.
And I have divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter arranged of the head into feet, in the forms of the tools and in the iron of the cauteries, and of that which is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of lancing and piercing, and of leeches, and of plasters, and of extraction of thorns and similar to those, all headed and arranged in the forms of the tools.
The third chapter, of restoration and of dislocation, and of cure of torture and similar to those; a chapter arranged of the head into feet, and of the forms of the tools.
I will say the quality of the humors of that cautery and of the pains of it, and in what disposition cautery is prohibited.
Then I say the word of the quality of the humor of cautery and of the pain of it is long and prolix and subtle by science, and secret hidden; and already of it many wise have spoken, and from it are disagreed, and to me suffices a little of the words of them, fearing prolixity.
And so I say that cautery confers with all universal disposition, which is by matter or without matter, excepting two dispositions, which are cold dispositions without matter.
In the cold and dry disposition with matter they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial to it, and others said the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in disease which is by quality and by dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and convenient is that disease cold and dry be healed by that which is cauted and dry.
And he said the contrary of that, that by cautery there is made humor in all disease cold and dry that one sees in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humors to the disposition of fire, you rub the body of man dry.
And I say by the word of that, that experiment that already to me many times have discovered.
However, it is not advisable to venture into this field unless one is already experienced and practiced in the chapter on cauterization, and has knowledge of the variety of human complexions and the disposition of illnesses in their bodies, as well as the causes of these illnesses and their accidental effects, and the span of time during which they occur.
However, in other complexions there is no cause for fear, especially in moist and humid illnesses; indeed all doctors agree that cauterization is beneficial in these cases, and they disagree about the use of cauterization only in certain cases.
And know, children, that the secret of healing with fire cauterization and the goodness of it is that the fire is simple, its action affecting only the part being cauterized and causing little damage to other parts.
But the action of a burning medicine extends to other parts, and it strongly brings on an illness difficult to cure in those parts; it also causes great pain.
But fire, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, but rather exceeds the limits of what is permissible; and it has been proven to us through experience and long study of the art that it is true in all cases, except for those who have not yet understood the secret of using fire, and the quality of its action in the body, and its expulsion of illnesses by philosophical reasoning. If this had not been so, I would not have spoken to you freely about the secret use of fire; nor would I have explained in detail the nature of its action in the body and the method of its use for removing illnesses through philosophical argument.
And know, children, that they disagree about the best time to perform cauterization; and they pause during the springtime.
I say that cauterization is appropriate at all times, because the accidental harm caused by the delay in treatment would be greater than the minor harm caused by performing it too soon; and especially if the cauterization is for painful or necessary illnesses, strong and violent ones, which do not tolerate delay because it is feared that they will become worse.
And fear not, children; only the common people and foolish doctors believe that for every illness treated with cauterization there is a specific time of year when it should be done; they pause and wait for that time to come around.
And the reason for their belief is not as they think, because cauterization is not a medicine that alters the complexion and dries out the humors which cause pain.
However, cauterization is superior to medicine because of the swiftness of its action and the strength of its effect, and the grandeur of its Lord.
And it is possible that the illness will return at some time, depending on the complexion of the patient and the location of the illness, and the nature of the cauterization, and what has been prepared in the body through an excess of humors, and what has been acquired through food and similar causes; but if the illness for which the cauterization was performed was a subtle one in a part of the body with few excess humors and moisture, as in a toothache and similar cases, then it is possible that the pain will not return, although this may happen in a small way.
And the common people say that cauterization is the last resort of medicine; and they are right to say so, not because they understand it, but because they believe that there is no cure that benefits from medicine or any other cause except cauterization.
And the reason for this is the opposite; and the belief that cauterization is the last resort of medicine arises when people administer treatments for certain illnesses, but the medicines they use do not work, and so at the end they administer cauterization, which benefits the patient. And so it comes about that cauterization is regarded as the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron; and they did not say this because of the equality of gold and the subtlety of its substance.
And they said that the material used for cauterization makes no difference, which is not absolutely true, for I have seen that it does make a difference in some bodies, and cauterization with gold is better than with iron as they say; but when you perform cauterization with fire using gold, it is not declared to you because the strength of the fire depends on the amount of gold used, and because the gold prepares itself to receive the heat; and if the fire is applied to the gold, some laminae are formed which the artisan can see.
And so cauterization with iron must be done more gently and more carefully to maintain the correctness of the operation.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cauterization according to chapters, and I have ordered that the feet be treated first, so listen now to what I will say about it.
The first chapter:
This cauterization confers moisture and coldness to the nervous system, of which there are two causes; it is a cause of swelling and many rheumatic pains, called "corissa", in the parts of the head and around the ears, and many other pains, including toothache and jaw pain, and generally any illness caused by cold, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar illnesses.
The procedure for this cauterization is as follows: first, the patient's head should be cleansed with a laxative head poultice for three or four days, depending on the strength of the patient and their age and condition.
Then he should have his head shaved with a razor, and then he should lie down on his back on a warm bed with his feet raised above his head.
Then pause the palm of your hand over the bridge of his nose between his eyebrows. However, if the place where your finger points has become affected, mark that place with a tent and then ignite the cauterization olive; this is its form:
Then hold that over the marked place in such a way that it descends, and your hand should be pressed gently against it little by little as you turn it.
Then lift your hand gently and examine the place; if you see some of the head has been exposed, as much as the amount of bone that was discovered, then lift your hand again, and if it does not return to you with the iron, but with another one, if it is cold, between the time it was exposed to you.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it over the place for three days.
Then hold a cloth soaked in vinegar over it, and let that remain on it until the scale formed by the fire is destroyed. Then heal the place with the four-part salve after it has healed.
And so they say that when Roman plaster is applied wet, it is better and more effective.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized within the ear, and the cauterization should be applied where some part of the skull has been removed.
Then it is to be washed with vinegar until the blood stops flowing, and then healed.
And others say that the cauterization should come to the end, where the bone is broken, and that it should pierce the skull until it hits the bone; this is the form of cauterization when there is a small wound: And they believe that from that place vapors are exhaled towards the head.
If the wound is open for a long time, it should be healed by cauterization until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two species of cautery at all, except in some men according to a way of fear; and leaving that aside, it is better for health, as it is, because the head is devitalized due to the solution of continuity of it as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a phlegmatic nature.
And the species of cautery is more safe and better for me, and from that use and by that action health is achieved.
Chapter According.
Of cauterization of the head.
When in all the head there occurs chronic and long-lasting pain and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses garlic and onions and caputalgar and poultices and plasters and bandages and hot stones and woolen pads and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we have recounted and does not benefit from any of those causes, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong creation in its nature and is phlegmatic and finds great heat, then cauterize him with another cautery beneath that one a little. Then cauterize that on the horn of his head so that the membrane is cut. The membrane being cut and the quantity which we have recounted having been discovered and cauterized with a cautery in the part behind the head in the place which is called a foramen — and that is the place in which the windpipe is paused — And move your hand on it and do not uncover the bone because the patient will feel great pain from that unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I will tell you about this cautery in its place because the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occipital are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is the way to do it.
Chapter Third.
Of cauterization for new non-ancient hemorrhoids.
When a sudden pain occurs in the front of the head or a spreading pain enters the eye, evacuate the head with purging medicines and administer the other cure which I have described in the reasons for diseases; and if all does not help then cauterization should be done in two ways: either cauterization with an acute burning medicine or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take an alder twig and scorch it and smear the ends of it on both sides of the head. Then cut the place of pain in the temple with a pointed lance so that it penetrates into the hole beneath the membrane and is concealed.
Then press tightly on it with a bandage and leave that for three hours; then remove the alder and treat it with water and let the wound heal in two or three days, and place your hand over it while immersing it in hot water so that the spot makes blisters.
Then cure that with ointment until it is healed.
And if you want to do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron should be done according to this manner: heat up an iron cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a closed nail because its head is shaped like a nail with some curve and in the middle part of it there is a small protuberance.
Then place it on the site of pain and hold your hand there — so that the cautery underneath is not burned  — and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that is burned by the thickness of the membrane just like its size and raise your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned so that there is no blood flow.
Then soak a cloth in salty water and place that on the spot and let it be for three days; then place a damp cloth with hot water on it and cure it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you want, cauterize with the point of the scalpel according to cautery, and beware of incision into the artery in this new non-ancient hemorrhoid.
Chapter Fourth.
Of cauterization for ancient hemorrhoids.
When you cure hemorrhoids with what we have said about the preceding cure and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the cure does not help, and you see by the disease that it is insufficient for it what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or with fire, then if you ignite the cutellar cautery so that it turns white, mark on the site of pain with a line whose length is equal to its size or almost, and place your hand on one side and press tightly while you pierce the artery and let it flow into the bone.
However it is necessary for you to guard against the continuance of the mandible which is gnawed due to chewing and that the ligament not be burned and the nerve which moves that, and that no spasm occur.
And if it is about the custody and care of flow of blood from the artery that is cut, for there is danger in cutting it and especially to one who ignores what he does and is unskilled and inexperienced; let the operation be done at the beginning.
And I will remind you of the regimen for flow of blood accidentally from the artery according to its way in your own book.
And if you see this disease that does not yield to this cautery and the patient's body tolerates it, cauterize him in the middle part of the head as we have recounted, and cure the wound so that it is healed.
And if you want to administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter about stretching of the artery with cautery having two points, and this cautery is better and more effective.
Chapter Fifth.
Of cauterization for pains in the ears.
When in the ear there is pain due to heat and it is cured by lacractive causes and by the other cure which I have said in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then ignite the cautery and this is the one that has a point, of which this is the form:
Then pierce it and ignite it and let it be borne around the whole ear as a wheel is turned or in the circle of either one if the pain is in one. Lengthen it with the cautery of the root of the ear little by little so that you do not burn the spot with a touch.
And if the cautery is of ten points in the whole ear, and that one, then cure the places so that they are healed.
Chapter Sixth.
Of cauterization for torpor.
Torpor which is cured and by cauterization it is not but of the spices that come through phlegm according to what we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the species which occurs through dryness or by spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that species of torpor with herbs and head-purgatives and your cure does not succeed then when you cauterize the patient in three cauteries: the first should be of the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third should be the junction of two gums.
And apply your cautery to the contrary side of the sick person, because softening only occurs on the side that is healing.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize the patient with the cautery on the right side of the highest part of the ear, and on the temple another one, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger nail, and place your hand with the cautery until half of the amount of the size of a bean is burned.
And this is the form of cauterization, and it is specific to the knife-maker, the shape of which is described in front of the table. But indeed more subtle is that one a little so that you see it, and that it cuts through excessive thickness a little.
Then treat the area with what has been described in front so that it heals.
Chapter 7.
Of cauterization of ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you have treated it as we have said and your treatment does not work and the patient has no fever, then cauterize that with four cauteries: on all the horns of his head; and with cautery and the middle part of the head, so as we have said; and with cautery in the groin. And the manner of the cautery is done according to what is written above.
And it has been cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and it should be cured with what has been described.
Chapter 8.
Of cauterization of oblivion caused by flame.
Give the patient at the beginning large jars and pills of the servant purgative.
Then shave the whole head of the patient, and place over his groin an ointment of cedar described in the treatise on ointments.
And apply it several times, for that is the manner of cautery. And do that according to the order which we have already said.
And if he heals well from that, then it is good; and if not, then do to him three cauteries on the back of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger nail, and then cure the cautery with what has been described.
But if you think it is tolerable and the patient can take it, then do to him cautery on the middle part; and if you see greater tolerance, then cauterize that on both horns.
Then cure that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-colored according to the form described above.
Chapter 9.
Of cauterization of parasites and softening of all the body.
Give the patient purgatives for cleansing of the head as we have said; and then shave his head, and then cauterize that with cautery on the middle part of the head, and with cautery on all the horns of the head, and with cautery on the back of the neck, and with three cauteries on the spines of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body for parasites and the patient tolerates it and the sickness is strong, then cauterize that with four cauteries on the spines of the back, and lastly with cautery until half the size of a bean is burned, and raise your hand. Then cure that until it is healed, and let this be the cautery olive-colored.
Chapter 10.
Of cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized except for that epilepsy caused by phlegm.
And so, cleanse the brain of him at the beginning with herbs and with the other cure which we have described in the division when the patient is large and tolerates the exception of the medicines.
And if it is a child who does not endure the medicines, then administer warming and chewing remedies by the servant cleansing, before that for several days, with benefit of foods.
Then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize that with one cautery on the middle part of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery on the back of his neck, and cauterize each horn of his head. And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize that with cauteries which I have said, having paralysis and softening of the body on the spines of the neck and the spines of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-colored according to what has preceded it.
But if the patient is a child, then apply the cautery gently according to this manner.
Chapter 11.
Of cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humors and gross phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have said in having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is excess phlegm declining to melancholy and the body of the patient is moist, then give him to drink what cleanses his brain according to what has preceded in the division.
Then shave the head of the patient, and then make a decently twisted tallow over the circle, and then place it in the middle part of the head.
And let the patient be seated, and let him be held by all parts.
Then apply liniment; and it is more worthy of being boiled of old sheep. And boil that on the fire with moderate heat by measure, which he endures when he is in it. Then pour that into the middle part of his head on the circle, and let him remain until it is cool.
And do that with the patient in a week once a day, and let him have the other regime of him until he is healed; or if you want, cauterize that making small points according to his age, unless you have your hand with a cautery thus ordered: this is a special cautery which moistens the brain with equality.
Then let that be wrapped in cotton wool in a bandage or in gray flannel.
Chapter 12.
Of cauterization of water descending into the throat.
When it is declared that the beginning of water in the throat through the holes which you have said in the division, then begin and give the patient to drink what cleanses his brain, and guard him from all moisture.
And let that boil in a bath for a few days.
Then command him to wash his head, and cauterize that with cautery on the middle part of the head; and then cauterize that with two cauteries on two temples if it is the beginning of moisture in two holes, or of one side if it is the beginning of moisture in one hole.
Pierce with cautery all the veins and arterioles which are under the skin; and let the cauteries be long in that length of the temples.
Guard against bleeding when it is cauterized; and if you see from him bleeding, stop it with some medicine, which is to you possible.
And come with wisdom which is in extraction of arterioles and with pruning of those and cauterization of bleeding of blood.
And some bones are cauterized in the neck below two bones with two cauteries directly produced.
Chapter 13.
Of cauterization of old tears.
When tears from the eyes are old and have spread through the veins and arteries to a visible location on the head, and it is clear that this is caused by an excess of phlegmatic fluids, then cauterize with a cautery as we have described in the chapter on water-fever. Cauterize in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries below the temples, and with two cauteries below the bones. And if there is a need for an additional cautery, then cauterize on all sides of the eye socket at the top of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter XIV.
Of cauterization for a nose fetor.
When you have treated the patient as we have said in the previous chapter and your treatment does not work, then prepare and give the patient enemas for three nights. Then shave the patient's head and cauterize the middle of the nose with a cautery made from olive oil. Then cauterize the nose bridge with two cauteries below the eyebrows, just under the hairline.
And be careful not to burn the skin.
This is the form of the cautery, or nail cautery:
Chapter XV.
Of cauteries for softening eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery. This is its form:
And if you are cauterizing above the two eyebrows, then do so with small cauteries in a straight line, and have them extend from the temples. And let the length of each cautery depend on the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too hard with the cautery, but according to how much the upper part of the eyelid is swollen.
Chapter XVI.
Of cauteries for the eyelid when hairs are converted to thorns and punctures the inside of the eye.
When hairs on the eyelid become thorns that puncture the inside of the eye, there are two ways to cauterize them: with a cautery iron or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, tell the patient to look down so that his eyelashes lie flat, and then trim them, pulling them out from their roots while they are being cauterized. If the punctures hurt him, then strengthen his eyes with a bandage so that he does not blink while the hairs are growing back.
Then, when they grow back and curl, place the patient's head in your lap and draw the eyelid of his eyes with a tent, marking its shape according to the pattern of a dead leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
Then place cotton dipped in white vinegar or in a mullein tea over the eye.
Then heat the cautery, of which this is its form:
Then cauterize over the shape you have drawn, bit by bit, until the surface of the eyelid is burnt and appears like a dead leaf, all appearing healthy and proper.
And a sign of your work' s success is when you see the eyelid drawn up and the hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and leave it alone for three days, and then place a poultice over the area until the scab is healed. Then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if the hairs return for any reason and the eyelid is softened, then repeat the cautery over that area again just as you did at first.
But if the hairs are inside the eyelid, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position and curled back so that the hairs do not puncture the eye.
But the burning medicine cautery is done by telling the patient to look down so that his eyelashes are flat and then trim them, pulling them out from their roots while they are being cauterized. Then the shape of a dead leaf is drawn on it.
Then take one part powdered sage and one part live coals. I.e.: Take this mixture and make it into a paste so that it is not too dry; then spread this over the paper which was drawn in the shape of a dead leaf, and place it on one or both eyes. Place cotton dipped in white vinegar over the eye, and let the patient' s head be in your lap.
Then hold the eye over the medicine and press it gently without hurting him until he feels the burning of the medicine, for the burning is like that of fire.
And when he feels the burning, then he should let go of the medicine and press it with his finger; then when the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water. Then consider if you see the eyelid lifted as it is washed or cut, for then it is good. And if it does not return to being washed or cut, then do not put the medicine over that area again where the medicine did not take effect and there is no blackness, but wait until your operation has healed and the eye is drawn up.
Then place a poultice over it until the scab of the burning is healed, and then treat that with ointment made from defynikon or another one so that it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, due to your operation, to be very vigilant and careful that the medicine does not drop into the eye. And if the eyelid becomes soft after a few days and for some reason the hairs puncture the eye, then repeat your medicine over that area just as you did at first, and treat him until it is healed.
This is the shape of a dead leaf:
Know that men' s eyelids are diverse in size and smallness, and therefore your operation should be done according to that.
And do not take the way of certainty with one who is ignorant of this art.
Chapter XVII.
Of cauterization for a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you are treating a fistula as we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not work, then do this: tell the patient to place his head in your lap and let one of his servants hold his head so that it is not moved or shaken by him.
Then place cotton dipped in white vinegar or in a mullein tea over the eye.
Then heat the cautery, of which this is its form:
Which is hollow, thus in the shape of a straw or a bird' s feather, from one end where the cautery is made.
And if you see that it penetrates into the other end. And if you wish that it come out like a tent.
But what is hollow is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out of it pus and dried blood. And if it is not open, then open it and extract its hardness.
Then place the cautery which is well ignited over the fistula, and press it with your finger over the cautery in the nostril, so that your hand does not move or the patient be disturbed, and then place the cautery on the edge of the eye and bend it.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery it enters into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery one or two times if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place a poultice on that spot and bind it with a dry bandage until it heals.
But if ten days have passed and it has not healed, then place a sharp corrosive medicine on it until the bone is exposed and scrape it according to what is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cautery for a fistula, which some call the first: enter at the site of the fistula and plug it up, and then place in that crack a fine powder of which its formula is given in its chapter; and melt into that the quantity of half an ounce of lead; and hold your hand with the powder with good retention so that the molten lead does not reach the throat of the patient; and cover what you have placed over the throat of the patient with a poultice of yolk of egg or water, for the lead will close up the site of the fistula and heal it in a wonderful manner.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cautery and treatment, that is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal speculum and reduce the fistula into the cavity of the nose according to what will be given in its place.

Chapter 7.
On the cure of inflammation of the ears.
Some children are born from the bellies of their mothers not-perforated, and this happens in some men, inflammation in the middle of the ear due to pus or due to meat stuck in that; and this inflammation sometimes occurs in the hole of the eardrum which does not affect the brain, and sometimes it is in the external parts.
And that which occurs in the middle of it, which does not affect the brain, is in the major dispositions of difficult cure.
And in that in which you achieve the brain, you should hold the ear of the patient towards the sun and look at it. And therefore if inflammation is apparent, open that with subtle spatula of which this is the shape:
If the end of it in which there is a small breadth, of which one party be acute and the other not; and let the spatula be of two edges smooth so that it does not harm the ear.
And if the inflammation is due to flesh born in the ear or in the middle of it, then open that with subtle nails and pierce it energetically until all the flesh is torn.
And if the inflammation is in the eardrum, then take a slender rod of brass and warm it in the fire a little, and then insert that in the middle of the ear.
And therefore if you have sensed inflammation with that which the patient feels with ease due to the gravity of hearing, relieve the pressure of it with ease; and beware that you do not harm the nerves of the ear.
And then hold to the ear cloth of linen according to the quantity of the breadth of the hole which you have wound in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are certain of the hardening of the plaster, or take some and soak that in water and sprinkle it over that after it has been ground and use it; and if you see the ear already pustulated, then beware that you remove that which you have wound in cloth and replace that with another cloth which you have wound around cerot that is made of rose oil, until the pustule scabs away; and then cure that until it is healed.
But if blood flow occurs, then soak sponge or cloth in cold water and hold it to the ear; and use the other cure which constricts the flow of blood, which is said in the tract on distillation.
Chapter 8.
On the cure of athecal which causes dimness to the eyelids.
Athecal which causes dimness to the eyelids of men sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore hold those with tongs or with nails, and cut those with spatulas of their roots, and then hold it to the places ground.

And the best cauterization is then to cut off and to return the incisions several times when any cause of them remains; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burnt and do not return.
Chapter 9.
On the cure of grandeyssa which comes to the eyelids of men.
Many times it occurs in the eyelids of men some cause which is similar to grandeyssa in its strength and hardness, and therefore grandeyssa is called.
And it is an accumulation of dense humidity in the eyelid below and above.
And the appearance in them is that you should consider if the grandeyssa comes from that which appears of the eyelid and is swollen in all parts; and the cause of it is distant.
Therefore open that with a slit through the breadth, and then dig that in all parts with ease until it drains, and then hold that with tongs and cut off that.
And if the wound is large, then you stitch it up, and cure that within which is healed.
And if it is small, then you shall not be harmed through it because the blood of it heals itself and becomes flesh again.
And if the growth is declining towards the inner parts of the eyelid from within, then cut off the eyelid and suspend the growth with a thread; another option is that you make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is elongated by incision, then this is not noted in any cause.
Then wash the wound with salt water, and cure the site so that it heals within.
Chapter 10.
Of the cure of an ulcer in the eyelid above the wound.
Ulcers are grey in color and are engendered in the membranes of the eyelid from above, and mostly affect infants, aggravate the eyes of those and cause pain to their sight because of it; and they cannot bear to look at the brightness of the sun because of this.
And because of this they always squint their eyes, and sleep with their eyes closed; and so the eyelids are humid beneath the eyebrows, which have already been covered in inflammation, and the imminence of growth is apparent in the eye; and when you press on the location with your fingers, you feel growth within them.

But in the anterior cowl, you must split the membrane all the way through so that you arrive at the growth, for in the most disposed processes, the growth comes through the incision according to the form of whiteness.
Then pull it out with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers at the outer edges and revolve between the right and left hand until it is released.
And be careful that you do not pierce into the wound with the spatula so that with the spatula you may not come to the wound and loosen it.
But if you do not encounter arternach at the beginning of the incision, but you pierce into the incision a little with ease so that arternach may process; then pull it out as we have said; and then moisten the cloths in water and wine and place them over the site and tighten those with poweder.
And so that salt is scattered and placed in the cavity of the incision so that what remains of that humidity.

But if an ulcer occurs in the site, then cure that with soft bandages until it is healed.

Then let it be known that you put oil of the dog in which there is thickness, and wet in him words of a nosebleed, and place between each nose the quantity of a finger or less, and rub the patient's face and insert one extremity of the oil into the nostrils with the ray or for any other cause which it is possible to him.
And then having done this, let him breathe in his odorant so that it reaches the canals of the nostrils, and let him spit out onto his palm, for the children at school do the same, and it is a mild cause to him who wants to do so.
And then place the two extremities of the oil, one extremity that drips on the nose and the other that pierces into the nose, and then administer a cauterization of meat with the nostrils that are in the oil, and do this so that you know the meat has been cauterized.
And then extract the oil, and place in the nose after abscission of blood a cold poultice, and do this for three days or more until you see that it has healed completely.
And then at the end of the cause, place in the nose a lead pipe for several days until it has healed.
But if it is difficult to cure that which has been squashed, use it.
And this is the form of the instrument for cleansing nosebleeds with which are distilled oils and medicines in the nostrils:
And do this with silver or gold, which is like a little open lantern, likewise with little heat, and the meat likewise.
And if you want to, make a closed pipe likewise with reed, and the socket of the instrument for cleansing nosebleeds is flat, uncovered; to which there is handle on it, with which you hold it in him, pouring oil or what you want from the sucks and the causes sucking.
Chapter 25.
Of warts on the ends of the nose:
A brown spot appears on the ends of the nose, and it grows larger in a few days unless the patient is given a cure.
And because of this, you should cut off that spot at its very beginning and remove it all. Then place on the spot a cautery or burning medicine which will remain in place of the spot.
But if its growth has been delayed until it becomes hard and dark-coloured, with little feeling in it, then do not touch that spot with an iron, because such spots usually cause great harm to the person on whom they are.
And if the spot is soft and not dark-coloured, and its growth seems possible in all, then evacuate the patient and cut that spot out without fear, and cure the wound with something that will dry it and draw it together until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the stitching of the nose and the lips and the ears when their continuity is dissolved by a ulcer or a similar wound.
Know that whenever there is an accident of discontinuity in one of these cartilage structures, there are few who perform the operation successfully.
So, whenever for any cause, you find that there is a fresh ulcer with its blood, then bring together the two lips of the ulcer with a suture, and then cure it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the discontinuity of the wound are already separated and each has healed by itself, then cut off each part of the wound that is exposed until its blood issues. Then bring together the two lips with a suture and strengthen them, and over that sprinkle dragon's blood and fine-ground iron pyrites, and place on the powder some of the emollient plaster, or one of the other consolidative plasters, and let it lie for two days or three, and then take off the plaster and change the ointment, and let it lie until the scabs have fallen off by themselves, and then cure it with an ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of the stitching is that you put together the discontinuity with needles as we have recommended in the stitching of the belly, or put together the discontinuity with pitch as we have told you and make known.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those causes that come in the two lips on the parts within.
Many people have in the inner lips of their mouths hard warts resembling the skin of a hornet, and some are small and others large.
So cut through the lips and open up each of those hard growths all around, and then fill the opening with finely-ground sage until the blood is stopped, and then wash it with wine vinegar and cure the wound with something that constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the cutting of the meat which is attached to the gums.
Often there is on the gum a piece of meat attached which the ancients called albus, so you should suspend that with wax or have it held with tweezers and cut that from its root, and let the blood or sap run out.
Then place on the spot finely-ground sage or other constrictive or astringent powders.
And if that meat returns, then cut it again and cauterize it, for the cautery will not return.
Chapter 29.
Of the reason of teeth with an iron.
When in the surface of the teeth on the inner and outer lips and between the gums are attached rough, sharp, black or green spots which corrupt the gum and make the teeth loose.
So take a sharp knife and make an incision in the patient's mouth, and press on his head with your thumb, and scrape away the teeth where these rough spots appear, and remove their causes, which will resemble sand, so that no cause remains, and this will remove the blackness and the greeness and the other things until they are clean.
So if what is in those at the beginning of the cause is good, so much the better; and if not, then do the cause again on those on another day, and on the second and on the third until it comes to the final point where you want.
And know that teeth have need of many razors of diverse forms and shapes according to your operation. And therefore the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the inner lip is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, and these forms of razors should be prepared as follows:
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You should cure the tooth of its pain with every artifice and delay in complete eradication of it, for there is no cause that excuses it when it becomes visible as soon as it is eradicated, for it is a sublimated substance which causes excuse in its eradication.
But if the patient is forced to have that tooth eradicated, you must be certain which is the painful tooth, for many times pain deceives the patient and remains while the tooth is healthy, and you eradicate it and then the pain does not cease until the painful and sick tooth has been eradicated, for we have already seen this through operation of the barbarians.
So when you are certain which is the painful tooth, and that you know it, then cut around the circuit of the tooth with a chisel in which there is some vertidity, so that it is dissolved from the gum on all sides, and then maul it with your fingers or with fine pincers little by little until you concuss it.
Then put in it strong tenacles with great force or grip strongly, and press the head of the patient between your teeth which do not hurt him, and then pull the tooth according to rectitude in a manner that you do not break it, nor give the patient major pain.
And if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and put that down all around with ease and effort the removal of it in such a way as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or hollow, then fill that crack and tighten it with a subtle wire on the end so that it does not loosen when you apply your constriction to it while you are holding it.
And you should open widely with scraping around the circuit of all parts of the gum and keep your strength so that you do not break it, and return to the patient for the malice which is greater than the first pain of him.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their audacity about the eradication of them, for they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times great sicknesses come upon men which are very difficult to loosen, and their teeth are very hollow or all rotten, or they extract it with some bones from the mandible as we have often witnessed.
Then the patient's mouth should be washed with wine vinegar or wine mixed with salt.
And if blood flows from the wound, as well as several vesicles, then sprinkle some powdered vitriol over the wound and if it does not stop, cauterize.
The shape of the tweezers and thin tongs with which a tooth is extracted at the beginning should have a long shaft, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not bend when you are working with them.
And let the tweezers have such a thick shaft that when you are pulling with them they do not bend, and short shafts.
And let them be made of iron from India descending from straight shafts, and at the ends of those there are teeth which fit into each other and extract by strong compression, and sometimes the ends of those are in the shape of a stork's beak, which is hooked, and they are of strong compression.
Chapter 31.
Of the extraction of teeth and of the dislocation of the bones of the jaw when it is broken.
When you need to extract a tooth which is broken due to some cause, then you should apply a poultice over the site for one or two days until the site is softened; and then insert tweezers or tongs, the ends of which are like the beak of an owl called a stork, or hooked, in the shape of forceps:
The ends of them are made flat like a file or an alfileter.
And if it does not obey you with these forceps or tongs, then you should cut above the gum and uncover all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called a lever, a small one of which the shape is as follows:
And let it be short and thick at one end, and not bent so that it does not break.
Therefore if through this the gum is pierced, that is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the shapes: the shape of the first instrument is triangular at one end, in which there should be some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it is triangular and very thin at the end.
And you are helped with this instrument having two ramps, of which this is the shape, properly following: and with other instruments which are used in the extraction of teeth; for help is done with this instrument which is like a wild ox, and this is the shape:
of the triangular end bent or short, which should have some thickness, and not bent so that it does not break.
And another wisdom is that the instruments for teeth are many and similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and skilled artist in the art finds for himself materials according to what he understands from the operation and various illnesses; for there are illnesses to which the ancients did not give or assign proper instruments because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is dislocated, then let the foot be treated in its place with what is convenient for him from the instruments and forceps which we have said in the extraction of the gums of teeth; and help yourself with a gesture of which this is the shape:
And let there be some thickness added to it little by little, and let it be pressed or held in the mouth so that it does not escape or slip until the bone of all is softened; and then restore the site with suitable medicines for it.
But if there is a little dislocation in the bone, then scrape away that dislocation and its darkness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the cure of teeth which project above others.
When teeth are prominent not according to their natural form because of that shape, and especially when it happens to women, and especially to old women; therefore you should consider: if a tooth is adjacent to another tooth, and there is no possibility of lancing or lining it, then extract that tooth; but if it is not adjacent to another tooth, then you insert that tooth with this instrument of which this is the shape; and it is like the jaw of a pig, and let it be made of iron from India, of very acute form.
And let your insertion with which you insert that be in turns for the hardness of the tooth, and let it not be broken by another of the teeth.
And if the prominent tooth is worn away to a file, then file that with an iron file from India of which this is the shape:
And let all of it be of iron from India, and the edge evenly; and let it be of very fine points like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little in a few days with ease, so that no other tooth is broken or chipped. And then line it at the edge, and scrape it away with some razor.
And if a tooth of which one part is broken and lacerated the tongue, then let you smooth that until the roughness of that fracture is worn away and dulled, and let it be flattened so that the tongue is not cut or the speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of worn teeth with gold or silver wire.
When it happens to the teeth before concussion and removal by percussion or by falling, and the patient cannot bear what he eats, and you have treated that with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not availed, then because they do not close up, there is wire made of gold and not of silver; for silver becomes blackened and then dislocates after some days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the quantity which can enter between the teeth; and the shape of the restoration is that you take the wire and wrap it around the double of its width between two healthy teeth, and then that it grows with two tortuosity of the wire between two worn teeth, or if one or many, until it reaches the gum on the healthy tooth from the other broken part. And then tighten the wire around the part from which you begin, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not worn.
And let your tightening of the wire around which you tighten the wire, so that the teeth do not escape.
And then insert the ends of the excess wire with tweezers or tongs, and add those in every site with a gesture, and fold them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the worn tooth, so that it does not harm the tongue. And then let it be as tight as possible like Roman.
But if it is loose or jagged, tighten that or those with another wire in the same way at all times.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the restoration of two healthy teeth and two worn teeth, as follows:
And when a tooth or two return to their place after the falling of any of them in their sites, and they are restored as we have described, and they are worn again.
And it is not done except by wise artists.
And sometimes it happens because of some cause of the bones of a cow, and it is made like a tooth in form, and it is placed in the site where the tooth failed, and it is tightened as we have said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Of the beginning of the disease that affects the tongue and prohibits speech.
This condition that affects the tongue can be either innate or accidental and caused by a plague which has already become consolidated.
The operation on the patient is to open his mouth, lift up his head and elevate his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament according to the width through which the tongue has become detached from its attachment.
But if there is any hardness or knotting, and this is due to the consolidation of a plague, then you shall cut it with a knife and split it up to the width at which you split the ligament and the knot has been dissolved.
And take care that there is not made a fissure in the muscle of the jaw, and that you split up the artery and cause a flow of blood.
Then the trace of the incision is to be washed with wine or vinegar, or with water, and a linen cloth is to be placed beneath the tongue; if there is any swelling which the patient has under his tongue on any night and it has not become consolidated in another way, then this should be done.
But if blood flows, then apply powdered vitriol to the spot; but if it oozes out, then cauterize the spot with a cautery which is suitable for this purpose. And let that be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Of the extraction of a ranula beneath the tongue.
Sometimes beneath the tongue there occurs an abscess resembling a small onion, which prevents the tongue from performing its natural function, and it grows larger as it fills up the mouth.
The operation is to open the patient's mouth facing towards the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it of a dusky colour or black, hard, and the patient feels nothing when you touch it, then do not touch that because it is deadly.
And if it is white and moist, then put a knife in it and cut it all the way through; and if blood flows during the time of your operation, then put powdered vitriol on it so that the blood is staunched.
Then return to your work and treat it as you would have done if there had been no bleeding; after washing it with vinegar and salt, let the wound be treated with the other treatment for this disease until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
Of the extraction of an abscess between two ears, and that which is found in the neck, and of the other abscesses.
When there is swelling in the neck, it consists of glands which are united like cotton; these become inflamed and are named two ears, and when you treat them as we have said in the previous chapter, if they are not healed, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dusky in colour, with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red colour and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; before you leave it, either you will draw it out or it will break open by itself.
And if it is of a white colour, soft, and the root thin, then that is what should be cut.
And the operation is that you should consider before your operation whether the abscess has already been cauterized to completion or has decreased by any decrease; and then cause the patient, facing towards the sun, his head in your lap, to open his mouth, and the assistant taking hold of his hands press his tongue down below with a noble instrument, of which this is the shape:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be fine with a point.
Then when you press the tongue down with that and the abscess is shown to you, and you put your instrument over it, then take a knife and stab that in the centre outward as far as you can. Another which pierces through the tunics, and then cut that with an instrument of which this is the shape:
Another which pierces through the tunics, and then cut that with an instrument of which this is the shape: It is like forceps but its ends are bent inwards so that the orifice of each is towards the other, and it is sharp and made of iron, tempered and greased.
But if that instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a knife of which this is the shape: It should be sharp on one side but blunt on the other.
And after you have cut one vein, cut the other according to this method of cutting; and then have the patient guard his mouth with water.
But if blood flows, guard it with water of a decoction of bark of pomegranate or of bay-leaf in the same way as for the causes which are constrictive so that the flow of blood is staunched; and then let that be treated in the same way as the others until it is healed.
And sometimes there occur in the neck other abscesses besides those two ears, and cut them according to what you see and say about the cutting of two veins equally.
And there was once treated a woman of an abscess which was found in the neck, which abscess was dusky in colour and had little feeling; it closed the neck of her throat, and the woman breathed with difficulty and was prohibited from eating or drinking water, and she had reached death if she had lived for one day or two.
And the abscess had grown so large that it had reached to her two ribs on either side.
Then he cut it with a knife and took it out, and it came away as a great lump; then he opened the mouth of the patient, and pressed his tongue down, and then he put the knife in that abscess.
And then he cut a large piece out of it, and not much blood flowed from it. Then the neck of that woman was entirely closed up and she began to drink water immediately, and then he ate food; therefore he did not cease cutting that abscess many times over a long time, and afterwards another abscess occurred in place of it which he cut so that it is prolonged.
Then he bled and cauterized the neck of her, then she died on the spot; and afterwards he walked about for a long time but I did not know what God did to her.
Chapter XXXVII.
Of the cutting of the uvula when it is abscessed.
When mucous matter flows from the uvula and it is abscessed and long, then that is called perpendicular; and if at its base it is thick and rounded, then it is called a buba.
But if it is cured by what we said in the previous chapter and does not comply with your treatment, and afterwards you see that the abscess cauterized has become small and thin; and if it is towards the length, and is sanguineous, of dusky colour or black, and the patient feels nothing there, then fear that you touch the patient.
And when you see it according to the way that I have said of its whiteness and length, then make the patient open his mouth and press his tongue down with an instrument of which we have said the shape and put a knife in the new one, and draw that towards you and cut it with one of two instruments which are bent like a pair of tongs.
And you should not cut anything except what is over the natural limit, for if you cut more, you will harm their speech.
Then the incision, use that which we have recommended in the incision of two holes, and heal inside what is healed.
But if the patient was frightened by the incision, use trickery in his cauterization to reduce their fear, and the method of the cautery on him is simply a sharp instrument; then place the patient's head on your lap, and firmly press their tongue with the tool which we mentioned, then take the water which we mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with some live charcoal, and pour it into the hollow of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let the end of it be hollowed out like a spoon, and place the medicine inside as well as the concavity of the spoon, then place the instrument with the medicine over the wound; the patient should lie on their side so that saliva or mucous from their mouth runs down due to the intense medicine, so that none of it falls back into their throat and chokes them.
Then hold the medicine in your hand, and squeeze it onto the uvula through a space of about half an hour until you see it become blackened and numbed by the medicine.
And if you wish, take a needle and wrap it in the medicine at its end, then immerse the needle in the medicine and insert the needle with the cotton into the uvula until the cotton adheres to the uvula inside; do this as many times as needed until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, then let it be for three or four days until it dries up and falls off.
And if you see that the medicine needs to be reapplied, then do so; and after the cauterization, wipe the area around the uvula with a cotton swab dipped in a bowl of water, then cleanse it with whatever is in the bowl of water, then wash it with warm water, and dress the wound outside with ointment, and inside with unguents until it heals.
And this type of treatment is more safe than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is healed by something more subtle than cauterization or incision, for it is cured by fumigation in this manner: take calamus, hyssop, oregano, rue, Arabic thistles, chamomile, broom and similar plants to those herbs, and put all of these ingredients, or some of them, into a bowl, then immerse them in boiling wine; let the bowl be warmed with decent heat, and there should be a hole in the middle of the bowl on which to place an hollow instrument made of silver or copper.
Then insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the steam reaches their uvula through the tube, until the uvula is very foggy.
Then do this several times until it is dry; and beware of doing this cure at the beginning of an apoplectic fit, for it would push the apoplexy too far into the brain, and you should not do this, but wait until the apoplexy has subsided.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and fix a plug of wool at its end so that the patient's mouth is closed off; for the wool plug prevents the steam from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is beneficial to health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Of the extraction of thorns and of what adheres in the throat, and of other causes.
Many times something adheres in the throat or a thorn from the foot or some other cause.
Then you must extract what is visible from it, then press the tongue with an instrument in front of your eyes, so that you see what is in the throat.
And what is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food becomes digested in the stomach, for the cause of the throat often resides there; or the patient should swallow a piece of napkin or a ball of lettuce or a loaf of dry bread; or they could swallow a piece of dry sea sponge, and then chew it with their teeth, then they should swallow it, for when it reaches the spot in the throat where the thorns are, they will be driven out. Or take an eel and chew it with their teeth, then swallow it, or take a pig's tail and chew it and spit it out.
But if they are not driven out by those causes which we have mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this shape:
And it should be bigger than a small needle, and at its end there should be a hollow which you insert into the patient's throat with ease.
And let the patient hold their head up, and beware of touching their bones in the throat, so that it does not move them or touch the thorn or anything else; then let the doctor hold his hand there.
But if the patient wants to insert it themselves due to their knowledge of the location of the cause and they push it in and pull out their hand according to the amount that is prepared for them, then it will be driven out.
Chapter XXXIX.
Of the extraction of blood which adheres in the throat.
When the blood is stopped by what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and does not respond, then consider the throat of the patient when you press their tongue with the instrument which we have recommended.
Therefore if you see the blood, extract it with a small needle or a very thin and decently pointed one.
And if you are unable to extract it with that, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat; then place a hot iron in the hollow of the tube, and do this several times until the blood is dead and comes out of their throat; then the patient should abstain from all water for the whole day.
Then take a pitcher full of warm water, and pour it into their mouth, then rinse their mouth with it; do not swallow any of the water, just spit it out after an hour has passed, then hold your hand over their mouth again for another hour, for when the blood is dead, it will come out immediately.
But if it is not driven out by what we have recommended, then smoke the patient's throat with camphor or asafoetida through the instrument which we recommended for smoking the uvula.
Do this until it falls.
And there is another way to smoke it: take a bowl in which there are burning coals and the bowl should be covered with a lid that has a hole in it; then place the end of the instrument in the hole, and let the smoke out through the instrument; then hold the patient's mouth over the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the smoke does not escape until you know that the smoke has reached the blood, for the blood will fall immediately.
And if it does not work, then do the smoking many times; and let the patient abstain from drinking, and eat salt causes and eggs, and drink no water; and by this regimen it will be driven out.
This is the shape of the instrument with which blood is drawn from the vein when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps except that there is some curvature in it. which is placed on the vein, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is gloved, in which it is tightened with a file, so that when we press for any reason, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the speech in the opening of abscesses and their section.
Abscesses are of many kinds, according to what is remembered about them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncturing and sectioning in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and because we compress them by the moisture.
And the second way is by the places in which they occur in the body, for the abscess that occurs in the head is different from that in the buttocks; and the abscess in the fleshy part is different from that in the joint; and each of them is treated by operation.
And some abscesses there are which must not be punctured but rather matured in the pus in them, or completed.
And other abscesses there are which must not be punctured when they are tough, and not matured according to their complement, as well as abscesses which are near the joints; for an abscess which comes near a joint prolongs its being in that it affects the ligaments or nerves of that joint, through which there is contraction of that member; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its puncturing when it is mature it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, and one must not let it mature, but rather to puncture it, so that the pressure on it may not be prolonged and it elongated to the parts within the buttocks, and a fistula made, or that it be done in its term so that it will not be healed. But you must know the hour of the puncturing of the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
And you should know that it is mature when there is the ceasing of the pain and the fever, and moreover the lessening of the heat and the pulse, and the acute feeling of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because you should know the puncturing to occur in the place where there is pus below the abscess, and also pus coming from the healing of the eminence.
And the puncturing is always through the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the place of the tendons, of the cords of the nerves, of the grooves; and, at last, in all places alike in which there is no reflection; for in those where it is reflected, use puncturing according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, it is better to let its puncturing be so that its digestion is confirmed according to its complement, as we have said; for if you puncture it before that, the course of the pus of it is prolonged and much filthiness comes from it, and moreover its lips are hardened and its depth.
But some abscesses are punctured according to the latitude of the flesh or body by reason of necessity, or according to that by which the member needs.
And you should administer a small puncturing in small abscesses, and in large abscesses great puncturing and ample or many sections according to the diversity of the sizes of the abscesses.
And some are of abscesses in which the vein is hollowed out and cut into, when it is made thus so that bread and made is in term that it already is, as well as that which occurs in trops of dubelec and of buboes.
And of those some are that are cut into with three angles; and of those is the one in which it is cut as the shape of a leaf of mirtle, as well as the abscesses of the angle-makers.
And of those some are in which round section and lunar section is administered, and like those.
But that to which there is not head as with its abscesses superficial, you should puncture it with superficial puncturing, or plane only.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should puncture it and not be afraid to draw out all the pus in that hour; having drawn from it a part, then plug up the abscess for another day, and moreover draw a larger part, and do this many times according to degree until it is hole-gutted.
And especially if the patient is of cold nature, or woman in labour, or small child, or old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit many times is resolved by the egress of pus, and very often the patient dies, and you operate on them.
Therefore keep this chapter with great caution, and moreover your puncturing in which you puncture these abscesses, you should wash the plagues and consider then if the plague of the abscess is little, or a flat section, then you administer lambs' wool or old linen; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the puncturing are many, then you must pause a moment in each piercing until they come out.
And if in the abscess a part of the vein is cut into or hollowed out in its circuit, then you must complete it with old linen or thread made from cloth of lamb's wool, as well as when you have drawn pans or roots, without moisture; and plug up that for the third day; and moreover process it and heal it by what means of enguentz within that it is healed.
But if blood flow occurs in the hour of your operation, then you should administer water and wine mixed in which lamb's wool is dipped, and pause that over the place of the blood flow many times.
But if the blood flow persists, then you must administer remedy and healing, and powder which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the winter time, and the place of the abscess is the place of many nerves, then you should dip wool in wine and oil cautery, and pause that over the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy, then you should pause wool wetted with water and oil or wine and oil, and all that be rubbed within that it is the third day as we have said, and moreover you unbind the abscess and wash that and administer in its healing what to it befits within that it is healed.
And that is what is necessary for the knowledge of the healing of abscesses generally, but according to the way of distinction I have said of each abscess in what manner its way of cure is by chapters singularly.
Chapter 41.
Of section of abscesses which come in the vein of the head.
In the vein of the head small abscesses, and they are of species of knots which contain membranes that they are to them made; and their species is many.
And of them there are bumps, and some contain moisture similar to lime. And of those some contain moisture similar to dust, and like it; and of those some are scaly, hard.
And in the section of all these, and in the extraction of them there is not fear, as you must extract those it does not contradict artery.
And the operation in section of those is that you expect those with an instrument, the shape of which comes to light, which is called a trocar, while you know it contains.

And begin the section of the line from B to G. Therefore when the humidity is evacuated, then you should open that which contains the humidity, and not leave any cause behind, for if any remains, it will return.

But if your bowstring causes you difficulty, then do what I told you at first.
And similarly do with the suppurating abscess in terms of section and cure according as we have told you; and a section on a suppurating abscess is easier, for it contains little blood and little humidity.
And some times there are some of these abscesses in the head in certain people with which there is no humidity at all; the cause of that is when one has struck an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like dumb, rough, white stone, which could not be pierced, and if one hits it, it hurts.
But with the other accidents of abscesses in the head there is not this matter, as with the abscesses that come to the heads of children and from the roots of the ears; but the section of these should be superficial sections.

Chapter .XLI.
Of sections of scrofula which occurs in the neck.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck and armpits and angles; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur in the neck, and some are engendered between them, and all scrofula of these is within its own membrane, and similarly with the nodules and abscesses of the head as we have related.
And the species of these scrofula are many, for some are stony, and some contain humidity, and some are malignant and do not obey cure.
Therefore with that which you see of good disposition in the Touching, of which the colour of what is manifest is close to the colour of the skin, and is swollen throughout all parts, and is not annexed by the nerves of the neck or the veins or the bowstring, and is not deep, so that you open that with a superficial cut from above down from the part above the body from the line of G. between the line of B., and scorch that all over, and cut off its edges from two lips of the skin with an incision, or with many incisions if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and pull that out little by little.
And it is more careful that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And be careful that your hand not move with the incision or disturb the patient, and cut what is not necessary to be cut; for if you cut a vein or bowstring and forbid yourself from your operation, then apply to the wound vitriol triturated or other powders which stop fluxes of blood, and tightly bind the wound, and let that remain until the acuteness of the abscess is relaxed and the wound softened and begins to suppurate. When the blood is abstained.
Therefore return to your operation until you have completed it.
Then inquire with your finger whether any cause of the other small scrofula is left; therefore cut those and clean those.

Then fill the wound with old dung which you have already immersed in Egyptian ointment, and then cure that.
And if you cut that scrofula all the way through, then so that you press the two lips of the wound and suck it at once so that no superfluity remains.
But if you see that there is superfluity due to the size of the scrofula, so that you intend to cut its root, and open that and administer cauterization and what has been said.
And that of the scrofula which contains humidity, pierce that with a superficial piercing where you see to you the place of its maturity, and make the piercing of that which is lowest in the body as we have said.
Then administer cauterization, and apply ointment and similarly to those for so that it corrodes what is left of the corrosion. Then when you see the wound already cleaned, then cure that with ointment of generative flesh until it is healed.
Chapter .XLI.
Of section of the pustule of the suppurating abscess within the gullet.
They have not yet seen this section in the pilhot, and there has been no man in our land who that has done that.
And this is the inscription of the term of those.
But with men having scurvy, so that the extraction of the pilhot is avoided, for by that not being done there is not made ease for so that all the veins and the major nerves of the patient are here.
