The form of the instruments that are administered to give a cautery:
Sometimes cauteries are made of silver or brass or molten and hammered ram's horn; and sometimes they are made from small and large instruments according to those they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and of those whose anuses are sore or painful, their cauteries should be small.
The form of a large and slender cautery:
The head of the needle, above which the pouch is tied, should be made wide according to this manner; and let there be a distinction where the pouch is tied as well as the times.
And the end of it below, which is inserted into the anus, should be silent  (so that it is not open), drawn out thinly; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the width of the hole according to the thickness of the rays and more coarse one small.
And let the pouch in which are placed the medicines of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its root be one palm long.
And let it be pierced with many holes so that when it is rolled, the thickness of the finger is between the holes.
And then insert into those holes strong thread of hard gut which is made from ten guts, or wrap that around it; and fasten with that the pouch, and when the pouch is placed on it, tighten the head of the cautery between two distinctions with a strong constriction, and then cauterize with that each hole: However, the quality of cauterizing with that is that you fill the pouch with medicine, which should be warmed according to the amount of the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient should lie on his back, and raise his legs up and place underneath him a board or a pillow; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or mucilage of fenugreek or similar; and then insert that into the anus easily, and then take the pouch and squeeze it with both hands strongly so that everything in the cautery is evacuated; and then withdraw the cautery and the patient raises his legs against the wall.
And if the medicine forces him to defecate, he should hold it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with that for a whole night, he is better off.
However, if you cauterize him in the illnesses of looseness of the belly and his defecation stops, then you should repeat the cautery until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or abscesses in the anus, then let the cautery be very thin and sharp, of those cauteries with which children are cauterized.
Treatment of piles:
You have already said in the division of the treatment of piles what suited the free, and I have thus described their treatment with hand and medicine according to the complement.
Therefore say that piles are varied according to what they are made of and according to the location where they occur.
And the causes for which piles are caused are thus: a stone is lodged in the anus or the rectum is pierced by a spear or a staff, or it is struck with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar.
However, the piles that are in the body's locations are thus: a plague which falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the buttocks, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I have recommended the treatment of some piles that stop that reasoning and rule over the others.
And I will begin the piles of the head simple, for the remembrance of the compounded ones will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to a blow from a stone or something similar, and the skin has torn only slightly and there is a large wound and you fear that pus will form, then you must immediately open and flebotomy the cephalica vein, and do not delay this; and extract the blood according to the amount of its virtue if nothing else prohibits flebotomy, especially if the blood that flows from the wound is scarce.
And it should be a flebotomy on the opposite side of the wounded part; and the patient should be guarded against repletion, wine, and heavy foods.
But if some reason prohibits it, then let the food be diminished and do not bring him near meat or wine.
And place over the wound, if it happens that pus forms, a poultice soaked in rose oil or wine in which there is a styptic.
But if he is sure that pus will form, then place over the wound, if it is recent, his own blood and let the air not have changed that powder which I will shortly mention.
And if the air has already changed that, then place some ointment on it until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or it is due to a cut from a sword and similar weapons, and its lips are not joined by threads, then join them with stitches according to what I will mention about their stitches.
But if the wound is uncovered to the bone and there is no retention of it except for a small bandage, then insert into it and heal the wound with medicines and restore the flesh by applying in place of thread, and order the patient to abstain from foods whose nature is coarse: heads and formen roots and similar things to them, until it is composed.
And if you find in the thread putrefaction and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all of that rotten matter and throw it out, and heal it afterwards.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow and it is not severed by medicines, then probe for the artery and if you find that it is not severed, then pierce it with a spike, or ligature it; and if necessity compels you to do so, cauterize it until the blood is severed.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is lightness; therefore you should dust over those this powder, for it will solidify before the air changes it.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil a quarter part, dragon's blood two parts, and gum arabic three parts; let all be well pounded and sifted, and fill the wound with it, and apply it tightly so that it adheres to the wound and coagulates with the blood.
And then bind it tightly above with lint and let it be so while there is pus in the wound, for if you remove the medicine before it is healed, the wound will not be cured.
And sometimes it does this alone, when there are no dragon's blood or gum arabic at hand.
And this method of operation is also done on large wounds when it seems that it should be applied and the lips of the wound are joined together, and only on recent wounds whose blood has not been altered by the air.
And if the air has altered them, especially during the summer, then it is not necessary to apply this powder, but afterwards cure those with what we have mentioned, by applying over them some digestible ointments or pastes made from flour of barley with water and honey so that they form a poultice; and heal them with the other cure until they are healed.
But if there is a broken bone in the wound and it is small, then you should extract it with tweezers.
And I will tell of the cure for head fractures when they are punctured.
Of wounds in the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in its medicine, and it is similar to the medicine for a simple wound on the head.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there should be no incision made in it but rather avoided by applying medicines to it that cause violent contraction, as with cinchona and zinc which are like vitriol and similar things because they hurt nerves and constrict that place. And do not put over the wound anything causing any heat, for the substance of nerves is heat and their continuation is through the brain, which is the source of all the members.
And if it is being cured by medicines that are cooling, as with water that has been sprinkled with rose oil or not, and thucium when it has been washed with tepid water and the other stones according to this manner, then apply moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer stitches or tie together its lips, and heal it afterwards with lint until it is healed.
But if there is depth to the wound or there occurs in it pus with blood, then probe that place more deeply.
But if the artery is cut and blood flows from it, then stop or dry that place, or ligature it, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the spondylous bones of the neck or all of them are cut and the veins of the neck are saved, then join together the lips of the wound with stitches above the spondylous bone of the neck and do not touch that place; before you return it to its natural shape and bind it with decent tightness and let it be bound for several days; and heal it with ointments in which there is contraction and excitement until it is healed.
But if the wound is on the bone and there is depression in it, then probe it and if there are fragments of bone in it, extract them all that you can; and let what you cannot extract be left inside so that the wound becomes suppurative, and let its discharge be separated and the swelling of it alleviated.
And if the wound is recent then dust over it the powder that I have mentioned to you. But if there is a fistula in the wound, then heal it with what its memory will come to you afterwards in the next chapter.
If it is a piercing of a spear or staff and its depth, then consider also if from the wound there is windiness when the patient breathes, then know that the wound is lethal.
And if there is no depth to it and the wound is recent with its blood, then apply over it in the first hour powder, and do not bind it so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and weakens the patient; but apply over it attractive ointment, and let it be loosed two times a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then probe the wound's orifice with a stick so that it sucks what there is of moisture; and let the patient sleep on the wound so that it runs what is retained in it.
But if three days or more have passed since the plague and the patient does not experience any spasms, tremors of a bad heart, or stricture of the throat, and you see other signs of good health in him, then you may know that he is saved from the plague; and then cure his wounds with cupping and with other remedies until he is healed.
But if the healing of him is excused and all time is open, then you may know that a fistula has already been made; therefore, cure it through its opening.
And if the plague is from an incision of a spear or knife, and it is simple in appearance on the surface of the foot or leg, then cure it with water that has been preceded by sewing up the wound, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if there has already been an impression on the bone and you see that it is incising into pieces, then you must twist the plague and cut to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then let them remain inside so that the plague can be poured out; for their extraction is dangerous.
And as for other plagues affecting the other members, the cure is the same as what we have already said.
Of plagues of the belly:
The disorder that occurs in the belly is either great, or small, or middling.
And a small disruption is made difficult in another way, and one knows that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not cut off their intrusion into their place at once, for it opens and becomes swollen, so that the insertion of it is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is middling, for in that the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two species mentioned above.
And know that the sewing up of the belly is according to four ways; but two sewings are common and convenient in the sewings of the other plagues of the body.
And two other sewings are proper to the other plagues, but they are more proper in the sewing up of the belly.
I will now give you an account and explanation of each one.
Therefore I say that when the plague is small and has come from some cause of the intestine, and it is difficult to reduce it, then it is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when there has come inflammation of the intestine because of the chilliness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then you should warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or piece of damp cloth in water made tepid only with squinant, and cypress, and spic, and embrocations with that, and apply it to the intestine so that the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with wine in which there has been added camphor, and it is better than water alone for the resolution of inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which is added alder and mallow, for by that the insertion of it is eased by a little cause.
But if the insertion of it is excused after this cure, then split the disruption a little with an instrument by which are cut off hemorrhoids.
And the first cure is better when it is proper that there be no section and no occupation to dry up close to necessity and defecation.
Of the instrument, know its form:
One part of it is bent acute, and the other part not acute, and its extremity be subtle, and may not have the subtlety of a spatulum, but rather be obtuse a little.
And therefore when the plague is amplified and you see it outside the intestine, then know that its reduction should be according to the form of it natural, in its proper place, if possible on it, for that is better.
But when the disruption is ample and it is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on his back and place his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you place his head and feet higher than his middle parts.
And likewise if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the belly, then pay attention and your consideration always on that so that the part in which there is the plague be higher than the other.
And therefore you should administer this in great plagues and in middling plagues.
And in small plagues, pause what is prepared for you and let the minister be between your fingers, which holds the disruption all by his hand and tighten the lips of it, and let him discover to you that he knows the sewing up of it from any cause or another.
And this is the way of common sewing up: you take the needle or thread according to the amount of the amplitude of the plague; and let it be taken from the extremity of the disruption of the ring finger, and tighten the thread one turn, and then you do not put oil in the two extremities of the gut, with two extremities of the phalanx which is below the gut of the parts of fingers, inside that you make it to penetrate of that part; and you have already added two extremities of the gut and two extremities of the phalanx, and so you have made four turns.
And therefore tighten with oil double in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes of two parts together, and let it be added the two lips and the plague by decent agregation; and let not cease doing this if it is necessary with needles until you have filled all the contraction of the plague.
And let the needles be middling between thickness and subtlety, for subtle needles pierce the flesh more readily; and thick ones are of difficult insertion in the gut, and therefore let them be middling in thickness and subtlety.
And let the needle be fixed in two extremities of the gut nearby, so that the flesh is not pierced readily and the plague opens before its consolidation.
And do not be prolonged in sewing up so that the plague may not be prohibited from consolidation.
And let the extremities of the needles be inserted so that they do not cause pain to the patient as he wakes from his sleep; and you shall pause upon the sewing up powdered expanders of all parts, which hold the extremities of the needles; and let them be stopped when you know that the plague is consolidated.
And these species of sewing up with needles in this manner is more convenient in small plagues, for you have at the sewing up of them one needle or two or around those.
But the second sewing up is communal: and it is that you administer with sewing up the four extremities, of the gut and two extremities of the phalanx, in one go, with a needle in which is oil wrapped in thickness and subtlety.
And therefore when you have with the needle penetrated these four extremities, reduce the needle of that meted part from which you began, so that the oil withdrawn draws the pus from the more superficial part to the plague, so that the sewing up is according to sewing up of cloth by which the cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the amount of the thickness of your little finger.
This is how surgeons sew up wounds.
And with this type of suture I sewed up a wound which had been inflicted on a man in his belly, and it was a wound two fingers wide, and the rupture was of the size of a palm, and it had come out of the intestines about two palms from the anus; and so then I reduced that after the intestines had been outside for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was healed within fifteen days or so, and he recovered and returned to his normal self.
And other doctors had judged that he would not recover; and by miracle it healed, because I did not cure it with ointment, but instead placed a piece of cloth twice a day on the wound and washed it often with honey-water until it healed.
But I will tell you about two types of proper sutures according to the account of the Word.
And it is so that you begin with the stitch of the thread, and you insert the needle from outside to inside at the beginning.
And then when the needle penetrates the thread and that end goes in according to rule and along the length of the entire body, leave the tip of the ciphac in which you have inserted the needle; and let the other tip of the needle penetrate from inside to outside at the other end of the wound.
And then when you have made that one penetrate, then make the other one penetrate again at the other end of that wound from inside to outside; and leave the tip of the ciphac which is on this side, and let the needle penetrate at the other end from inside to outside.
And let that one penetrate with the penetration by which you make it penetrate into the ciphac at the end of the wound that is on his inside so that everything penetrates.
Then begin from this side of the wound and sew that with the tip which is of the ciphac on the other side, and pull out the needle of the thread which has penetrated, and then return that into that thread and sew the tip of the ciphac which is on the other side with this end of the wound; and pull out that of the thread which is on his part.
And do this once and then again, and do this once more until you sew everything that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this method of suturing is that you sew with the stitch of the thread by which you sew the skin, so that you sew the ciphac one time from the end of the thread, and then let it be on the other side so that the edge is completed.
But the second type of proper suturing which I describe is the way of Gualia, and this is from the same matter as him and recommended by him; it is that you sew that according to semilitut with which some doctors sew it, in such a way that they join every part to its equal part in him conveniently by nature; and apply one end of the wound to the other end of the wound, and this is done according to what you judge.
Put the needle into the end of the wound that is near you from outside, and make it penetrate to inside in this one, and leave two tips of the ciphac; and then turn the needle and let it penetrate from inside to outside in two tips of the ciphac.
And then turn those ones, and let them penetrate from inside to outside in two ends of the wound that are on the contrary side.
And this method of suturing is better than the common lighter suturing, and it is the suturing of which I spoke before.
This is the way of Gualia.
And some said that the suturing of the belly is rectified with two needles, so you insert into these one needle at a time, and begin from where you are supposed to, and let it penetrate into the other side according to the way those who sew the body with the mouth sew.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle part of the belly, then the suturing of it is more difficult than of other parts of the belly.
But the judging of medicine for a wound is judged according to other wounds.
The cause of this is because when you find the wound still fresh with its blood in front so that the air does not alter it, and reduce the intestine and sew that and dress it decently, then place on it a carnificative powder.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments which are to you known so that they make a hard stone and draw the threads, and let the ciphac and wound be consolidated; and then cure that in this way as is the curing of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then place on it immediately, because as soon as pus begins to flow out, change that two times a day, and let the patient move two times a day, and take care of him until he is healed, for you will not need other medicine in many dispositions when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will communicate to the main members, then take wool of lion in oil of good quality, or in rosated oil, and place that around the places which are between the angles and under the joints.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in your intestines, which it should, then anoint that with black styptic wine, and especially so if the rot is caused by the intestine and there is a penetrating opening into its cavity.
And know that what is of the gross intestine is easier to heal than what is in the subtle intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called “gegunu” does not receive healing from a wound that boxes in it from all sides; and this is due to the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of its size, and because of the subtlety of that one, and because of its proximity to nature of nerves.
But if what comes from the wound is the thread, and you obtain that fresh, then reduce it according to your rule by which you reduce the intestines in the same way.
But if it has come to that space and is already green or black, then let you strengthen that with thread over the place that is blackened in it, because of the flow of blood, for there are veins and arteries in the thread.
And then insert that which is other than that one, and let two ends of the thread hang from the end of the wound outside it, so that on you be eased the attraction of it by means of the seating of the thread and emission of the pus of the wound.
Remembering of the wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When disruption happens in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some people, therefore because I saw one who had been wounded in his belly by a lance wound, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and made a fistula, and he vomited and had ventosity.
Then I followed the curing of him until it was known that he was not confident in his healing, but continued to reduce that until it was healed and the place was consolidated.

And I have already said some experienced people that when the wound is in the intestine, and it is small, then we must sew it according to this manner: and you should take pieces of skin from large animals; and then join together the two lips of the wound and place one of those pieces with an open mouth over both lips of the wound; therefore, when it takes hold of those and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not dissolve.
And then place another worm nearby the first one, and do not stop doing this with worms one after another according to the quantity of the whole wound.
And then return that one and sew up the wound; and those heads attached inside will remain until the intestine is healed; and nothing harmful should happen to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be tied with a thin thread made from an intestine of an animal attached to it, because it is thinner than a needle; and you should take the end of those intestinal threads and make it very thin, and then tie the end of it with its own thread, wrapped around, and pierce that thread through needles and sew it up with that thread, and return that one to its disruption.
And this manner of stitching with worms is not anything but according to the way of confidence and hope.
But if the wound is big and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is nothing but thinning, and not complete healing for it.
Of treatment of hernia and fistula.
Know that any wound and pustule when it becomes old and ancient, and is made into an ulcer, and is not consolidated and oozes pus all the time and is not healed, then, in whichever part of the body it may be, universally, it is named fistula, and we call it hernia.
And fistula according to truth is hard, white knotting, without pain, and to which there is a hollow like the hollow of a bird's leg; and because of that it is named some knotted, that is, pain.
And in some people it is moist, which oozes pus; and the moisture is cut off in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is increased, and is less, and is thick, and is thin.
I have already shown all those causes and their signs and the treatment for them with medicines in the division.
And so I will make you know the treatment of hernia which is made by way of operation with the hand.
And know that hernia happens in all the parts of the body; for there are hernias which happen to the large veins and arteries, or to the nerves, or to the brain, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints composed by composition like that of the hand and the foot; and those are near to the principal member and similar ones.
And therefore that one of these hernias which is according to this manner is of the evils of difficult healing, and does not receive cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.

Of cure of sores and quality of cauterization with medicines.
When apostemas occur in various members of the body and the space of the apostema is prolonged as it collects pus, then it is broken and perforated, and the pus is discharged from it, and the place is washed clean, and there is not inflammation on bone or on nerve or on ligament; it is called a sinus, and it is not named fistula.
But if the space is prolonged as corruption prevails in any member, then it is called a sinus and not a fistula.
And its cure is that you cut that codena completely as it is rolled, and more so when it is made with a saw, and compresses that do not adhere to the affected location by its corruption.
But if you hope that the codena adheres and that corruption does not succeed by other consequence, and in the codena has been a hollow, then cure it with a plaster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and if it is large, and the arm that you see gives off an offensive odor, then cauterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and wine agreed and received parts equally in a vessel, and are placed tightly over the fire so that the medicine is made hot and poured to specks of honey.
And then you take what is necessary from him and dissolve it with water and honey; and enema the rectum with it, and strengthen its orifice, and leave the medicine in him for the amount of two hours.
And then extract it with a syringe; and do this for as many days until the rectum is cleansed and the modesty restored.
And sometimes it is enema'd with water of ashes when you do not have this medicine at hand; and it is that you take the ashes of wood, and put water on top of it; and then stir it and enema with it until you are certain the rectum is cleaned out.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor water of ashes, then enema with water and honey mixed with ground cinammon, and also with wine and honey mixed, for the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and the wine causes adhesion to the rectum, and especially when there is excess of dryness and stippling in the wine.
And when you do this several times and are convinced that the rectum is restored, then enema with something that makes the flesh grow in it.
And it is that you take the thin unguent and dissolve it with rose oil and stout wine; or you can enema with some other medicines and unguents which I have already written about in the treatise on unguents.
And if the orifice of the rectum is tight and does not receive the cannula of the enema, then you should widen it with a small iron gradually, and place cautery wrapped in unguent Egyptian or unguent Basilicon on it until it is widened.
And similarly if the orifice is wide, then you should sew up the lips of it and leave a quantity in which the enema can be inserted without addition.
And if the orifice of it through which the bowel runs is raised up to the parts above, then dry that in the pus base place that is in it so that the bowel runs down inside, for when the bowel is constricted in the rectum, flesh will be generated in it.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the rectum all around in the pus base place as you wish, then you should make an incision so that you prepare the member with preparation by which the bowel runs slightly more easily according to what you have experienced and that no cause of the bowel be retained in it.
But that which is over the orifice of the rectum, which restores in all hour by itself that which is stuck in it of the bowel. And so that you know if that which runs from it of the bowel is little or much, or is digested, or not digested:
And with this consider the place of that rectum, if in it the patient feels pain or not, and if there is abscess in it.

And if from the rectum for several days bowel of undigested food is discharged, then know that the rectum does not adhere; and if the adhesion of the rectum is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you temper in the dryness of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dried thus with thin unguent when excess of addition of stiptic is in it.

Of treatment of panaricus and nail scraped and digit addition and section:
Panaricus is an abscess which arises below the nail of the finger of the hand or of the foot, and it is very strong in the other fingers, and when it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, or it is an abscess of cauterisation, or it is corrupted, and causes bowel to flow that corrodes the roots of the nail, and very strongly corrupts all of it, and very strongly pervades its corruption to the bone within which it has a fetent odour, and its extremity is made dark, and its colour is made dark.
And therefore when you have that according to what we have said in the division and your cure does not come about, then see that you incise with iron all of the excess which is swollen from the nail and then cauterise the wound, for cautery benefits greatly in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail are closed and compact, and they begin to press and sting it, then see that you pause a subtle ray below the angle of the nail which presses the flesh, and drive that up and insert that flesh with facility, and pause over that which exudes from the flesh of the medicines scorching within so that all is restored.
But if the corruption has already pervaded the bone, then see that you incise the bone and extract it, for there is no time in which the bone corrupted is healed.
And if the decay and corruption go to the finger, then you insert that in one of the knots according to how it is premeditated, and then cure that so that it is healed.

But since it is found in some people that there is very little flesh and very few bones, or very little nail.

And with those who are born fleshy, the cut is easy and you should insert it into its root with a wide spatula.
But that which is born in the root of a joint, its cure is difficult; and therefore beware of cutting it.

From an incision of a vein, they are varicose.
Varicose veins are varicose and are thick coiled veins full of melancholic superfluities that come in the parts of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and carriers.
Therefore, you should administer a purgation of the body with black bile several times with a strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But their cure is according to two methods: the first is that it is incised and that black blood is drawn out; the other method is that the vein is elevated and all of it is extracted.
The cut is according to this method: soak the leg first with hot water so that the thick blood is dissolved, which is feculent in the leg, and then tighten the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh towards the lowest part of the groin with a bandage, and then dry the vein at one, two, or three places with a wide cut, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the lowest part of the leg upwards, inside and out, until the quantity that you want and can endure is expelled, and the strength of the patient permits.
And then bind that, and order him to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this affects the patient.
But their elevation is according to this method: you race the leg of the patient if there are pains in it, and then put him in a bath and soak his leg with hot water so that he becomes red and the veins become swollen; or that he be exercised by strong exercise, if a bath is not available, so that the member becomes warmed.
And then dry the vein on the right side of the leg by cutting it in the long or behind part of it, or in the lowest part of it below the ankle.
And then open the vein with nails and gouge the vein from all parts where it appears to you, and its appearance should decline to blackness.
And therefore, when you see that white and chord-like substance, then you know that the vein is tied.
And then you put it below the thread so that it is elevated and let out of the vein, and suspend that with a lion-skin thong.
And then dry another cut, and then dry that cut through three fingers, and gouge the vein from above where it appears.
And then elevate that and insert it into the posterior cut below the ankle, and then extract it and elevate it until it is expelled by the second cut, and then extract it from the cut above that.
And do this until you extract it from the highest or uppermost cut of all the cuts, so that all is expelled; and then insert it.
And if it does not obey you in extracting and elevating, then insert a strong bent needle into it and ligature that and extract it, or insert your hand below the thread and turn your hand all around where it is inserted. And beware that it is not broken; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to elevate that motion and it has come upon the patient through that harm.
And when all of it is expelled, then place over the site of the plague wool soaked in wine and rose oil and not-rose oil, and heal that until it is healed.
And if the vein is contracted having tortuosity, to which there is involution at its parts, and it is not according to rectitude as we have said, then you should gouge that in all of its tortuosity and its involution, and then suspend that with nails so that you elevate it all.
And beware that you do not cut the vein and plague it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in that.

Of extraction of the leg vein:
This vein is generated in the legs in warm lands as in Al-Ateg in the warm land of Arabia and in the hot and dry lands of India, where there is little vegetation and few trees.
And it is very rarely generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction which comes below the vein as venom from serpents and worms and maggots and worms engendered between the vein and flesh.
And the sign of the occurrence of this vein is that there is violent inflammation in the leg and then the place becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to issue from the place of that blister which seems like the root of a plant.
And when his extremity appears, then you must wrap a piece of lead weighing half a drachma around it; and hang the lead from the arm by means of a thread, and let it rest on the chest.
And as many times as you see his eyes go outside because of something, wrap that up in lead and put it on over there. And if the swelling persists, then you insert a piece into him and wrap around the rest; but do not insert that part which was on the outside because it will be constricted and go inside the flesh and cause inflammation, pus and a bad ulcer at the site.
Because it should be treated gradually and extracted little by little, until it is pulled out completely from the rear of him so that no cause remains inside his body.
And from this vein, there are some people whose length is up to 20 or even 30 palm-widths; and I have seen one that was over 50 palms in length.
And if it is cut into during the time of its medication, then insert a needle into the opening and pierce it with a long needle throughout his body until everything that is in the material is evacuated, and cause putrefaction of the site for several days using medicines; and then cure that through the treatment of ulcers.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and comes to him many openings, and is extended from each opening.
And so cure that as we have said in the division and with what is above said.
About the generation of worms under the skin, and it is called bovine disease.
This disease is named in some lands bovine disease because many times it affects cows and it is a worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes through the whole body pushing and going down, and it is revealed by its movement from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is through putrefaction of certain humors just like it happens with worms and snakes and earthworms and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice because when it goes through the body and rises to the head, then it breaks out violently and goes in and destroys the brain, and that is why many times it happens.
And so when you want to cure that and extract it, then it is not done unless through his walking and appearing from him; and then when you see it above him and what is under him with strong constriction, then dry it out on him and extract that.
And if it is buried in the flesh and you cannot pull it out, then put fire on the site where it is hiding; and the main reason that is feared is corruption of him by which he corrupts the site just as we have said.
But if you see that it has reached the head close to the brain, then constrict on his forehead with a strong grip and dry him out there and extract that; and if the patient needs cleansing of his body with medicines that evacuate putrid bad humors and guard himself from foods that generate putrefaction.
About the disease of children.
This disease is named in our land a pain, and it is a pain that comes to some members, and then is transferred from member to member.
And you will see that according to what I tell you. I was called to a woman patient at a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation on the vein of the arm, and as pus came out for an hour, I saw that inflammation go like a worm pushing, but slowly, just as if it were a living silver when it goes from place to place.
And then it went into the pain of that site and settled in the muscles, and then I applied a plaster there for an hour.
And then it went throughout his body until it reached his other arm.
And I saw it move throughout his body just as I saw it, and was very surprised by its movement from member to member. And I had seen this before in my own eyes according to that manner; but I have seen many which cause pain being transferred from member to member. But I did not see that with the eyes just as in the woman.
And I did not think that it was because the woman was old, with thin skin and veins showing through, and in this she appeared to me that winding movement transferred from place to place.
And it is necessary that this does not appear according to this backward-cure in those who are delicate and with thin skin and veins showing through.
And when you want to cure that and the patient feels the pain, then do not put your hand on the opening as we have said above, but constrict it and pull it out slowly and dry it out there until that winding movement is stopped, and cauterize the site.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then cure that through expulsion of the body and by causing that winding movement to happen, and take out those by foul-smelling pills and pills of serapium and similar things.
About extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their species, and according to the parts of the body where they hit, for the diversity of them is that some have great points, and small ones, and hooked ones, and blunt ones; and there are those that have three angles, and there are others that have tongues.
And the signs of the liver are when it hits that arrow and pierces the bone and is stuck in the cyphax which is above the brain, for then occurs violent soda, and scotomia, and vertigo, and heat in the eyes, and inflammation and heat of the tongue, and spasm and mixing of the mind and vomiting of bile, and a strong loss of speech and destruction of voice, and moisture comes out from the wound site like white pus, and you see him like bloodless.
And so if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and extraction of the arrow if it is not out.
But the signs of the arrow when it hits the heart are that it is near the left mammary gland, and if it is stuck in a hard cause, and not a soft one; and then the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and it draws out black blood from the wound site, and causes shivering of the limbs, and fainting, and syncope; and then you know that death is certain.
And the signs when it has wounded the lungs are effusion of bloody spume from the wound site, and the blood vessels that are near the throat are swollen, and the color of the patient turns pale, and he breathes heavily, and asks for air to breathe.
And if he pierces the arrow into the veil that is on his foot, and the pain is then near the little ribs, and the great vein is made with great pain and sighing, and all the parts of the muscles are not painful.
And if he pierces the arrow into the stomach, then very soon you will see from the plague some undigested food.
And if he pierces the arrow into the belly, and it is stuck in there, you will see some slit of the plague, or the fabric, or hernia which is torn, and there is no hope for the cure of him without extracting the arrow.
And if the arrow pierces into the bladder, and urine flows out of it, and some cause processes outside the parts, and there is great pain on the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members like the face, neck, and throat, and shoulders, and the joints of the back, and the collar bone, and the hip, and their similar parts, are saved according to the greatest dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I will tell you what I have seen about the nature of these arrows so that you can understand it and use it for your cure.
The reason is that in a man who has been shot with an arrow in the tear duct of his eye, and I extracted it from the other side, after cutting through the bone of his nostril, he healed and did not lose the use of his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who was shot in the bone of his nostril, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already concealed and he did not get it out except for a small end with which it adhered to his face, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion's claw at its tip, and there were no nostrils.
And the Jew healed, and his eye did not suffer any evil accident.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man who was shot in the throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and that one to which the bone was attached; and then I entered between two bones with it, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I made it thin with that, and then extracted it, and the Christian healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who was shot in his belly, and I thought he would die from it; and then, since more than thirty days had passed and there was no change in him about his dispositions, I entered into the arrow and worked on it, and he healed, and no evil accident befell him.
And I saw a man in whom an arrow was shot into his back, and the plague solidified on him, and after fire had been applied to it for seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman who was shot with an arrow in her belly, and the plague solidified inside her, and she did not change in her dispositions in any way, and the woman did not suffer harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow was shot into his face and the plague solidified, and the arrow broke, not causing him any pain, and similar things happened here.
And I extracted the arrow of the king's son who was shot in the middle of his nose and had already declined to the right a little, and the whole arrow was concealed.
And I was called for his cure when the arrow entered three days ago.
And then I found the plague of the arrow very tight; and then I inquired into it with a very thin needle, and he did not feel that; but he felt a sting below the right nostril, and then I perceived that this sting was from the tip of the arrow.
And I applied the place with ointment in which is verityative and digestive, believing to me that the location appears to me to be the location of the arrow, so that it dries on it.
And there happened no significant incident to the location that the arrow had reached that location; and I continued with the ointment on it for many days, and there happened no accident; and the plague consolidated in the meantime, and the throat of the extraction of him healed over a space of many days until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and made me aware of it.
And then I applied acute corrosive medicine to the plague for many days until fire opened it up, and with a probe, I probed that, and found the tip of the arrow sticking in the bone.
And then I continued the perception of the plague with that acute medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I passed through time with that for the space of four months.
And then when the plague was swollen, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and remove it, without resistance, I did not cease probing and working on that with various tools, until one day I began on that with tools of descentment, according to the form of those in the end of the chapter, and extracted that; and then restored that plague.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose was not restored, and I restored that and the plague consolidated, and the throat healed with entire health, and there happened no evil in him.
And I recommend to you the quality of extraction of some arrows so that you pause this rule and significance about what I have not said, for the parts of this art and the definitions of that are not comprehended by narration or understanding, nor does it comprehend those the book.
And the wise artisans compare a little about what is moving about what is absent; and find operation new and tool novel according to the descentment of the causes strange of the art when it happens.
And therefore I say that arrows are not wrenched from the parts in which they are stuck but according to two ways, either by attraction of the location in which they enter or by the contrary of the other part.
And that which is wrenched from the location from which it entered, or is arrow proceeding in a fleshy location, and is drawn out and wrenched.
And if it does not yield to pressure of the hour in which it entered, then know that you leave it for some days until the flesh around it dries out and is loosened from its entrance and exit.
And similarly if it is stuck in bones and does not yield to you, then let it go for some days and repeat that with attraction and motion on each day, and it is wrenched.
And if it does not yield to you after some days, then know that you bore into the circuit of the arrow in that bone of every part with a very thin borer until the location is enlarged, and then extract that.
But if the arrow is hidden in the body and hidden from the senses, then you should probe that area with a pointed instrument that is suitable for extraction.
And if you cannot reach it due to the tightness of the flesh and the length of the arrow, and there are no bones, nerves, or veins in the way, then you should cut into the flesh so that it is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if there are ears attached to it, then you should carefully loosen the flesh around the arrowhead, using all your skill; and cut around the arrowhead, if you cannot extract it through the opening in the flesh created by the fracture of the ears and by twisting them so that they loosen.
And when you are extracting the arrow from any location, you should use your hand with forceps in every way possible to make it loosen; and gently with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and it is easy for you to extract it.
And if you cannot remove it immediately, then you should leave it for a few days until the tissues around it have healed; and then try again when the time has passed. But if you are facing a flow of blood, then you should do what we have said about healing in its respective chapter.
And guard against damaging any veins or nerves, or the bowel; and use your skills in every possible way to extract the arrow, doing so with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness as we have recommended.
And when you are contracting the arrow to pull it out, make sure the patient is in a position that will facilitate its removal according to its direction and angle, as that is more beneficial. But if that is not possible for you, then do what is possible for you based on your knowledge of the patient's anatomy.
But the arrows that are shot in the opposite direction of the other side, or those that have exited through the opposite side, or cause a bulge at the tip of the arrow from the deeper part of the wound, then you should press on it; and cut it according to how far the forceps can reach; and then pull it out, as it is easier to remove.
But if it is stuck in the bone, then you should twist the blade according to its angle so that the arrow cuts into the bone, and enlarge the wound, and then extract it.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days, and then try again when it has become loose.
And if the shaft of the arrow is in it, then you should push on it.
But if the shaft is broken and you want to administer pressure, then you should insert it into the arrow's groove so that it fits snugly into the concave part of the arrowhead and then push on it.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you should push on it with a strong instrument inserted into it, as the arrow will be loosen by that action.
And if the arrow is stuck in the wound, then you should cut away all the flesh around it that is possible for you, and then heal it as best you can.
And if the arrow pierces the foot, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side, and is close enough for you to touch it with your finger, then you should cut away around it and guard against damaging any veins or nerves; and extract it, and then sew up the wound if necessary, and heal it.
The shape of the forceps used to pull out arrows:
They are shaped like the beak of an eagle, serrated so that they grip the arrow tightly and don't slip.
And they are made in various shapes: large and small, wide or narrow, depending on the size of the arrow, the depth of the wound, the width of the opening, and how tight it is.

First chapter.
A summary of the nature of broken bones, from which a knowledge is necessary before we begin to discuss in detail the healing of various bones and parts. Now let us present at the beginning of this chapter, summaries of the subject that the reader must know and understand before we begin; and these chapters are for those artisans who have already achieved mastery and understanding of the truth about these matters, and also for you and others who will learn or perfect this noble art.
And so I say that when there is a fracture or dislocation or twisting or displacement, you should first apply leeches or other remedies to the affected area, or some other treatment, if no counterindications are present; also according to the strength of the patient, whether it be an adult or a child, and also taking into account any other conditions such as being in a state of weakness, or the age of the patient, or the season of violent heat, or violent rubbing.
And you should also be careful with food: eat fresh herbs and the flesh of birds and crabs; avoid fatty meats and rich foods that fill the veins with blood, unless you are sure there is no risk of pus forming in the affected limb.
And then return the patient to their normal routine, and correct any deviation from it that may have occurred.
And so when the broken bone begins to heal, then you should feed the patient nutritious foods that are rich and heavy, and contain a lot of moisture; such as dishes made from wheat cooked in water, rice, peas, beans, and the bellies of cows, and pigs, and fresh fish, and fatty wine and similar beverages, and with this regimen the fracture will heal sooner and better.
And you should know that the bones of old people and children are not able to knit together and heal properly because of their dry nature.
But nature adjusts and unifies the broken bone from all sides by a kind of bonding process, by which it becomes larger and stronger, until it reaches its final state of strength and firmness, just like fire at the beginning, unless some counterindication forbids this; and no cause can prevent this from happening.
And for this reason it is necessary to feed the patient foods that are heavy and rich in moisture and substance; as we have said.
And you should know that the kinds of fractures vary according to the different bones affected: a fracture of the bone in the leg is different from a fracture of the bone in the head, and a fracture of the bone in the foot is different from a fracture of the bone in the hand; and likewise all other bones are diverse among themselves.
And now we will discuss each kind of fracture separately, in its own chapter.
There are different kinds of fractures, depending on the cause. And the kinds of fractures that occur in bones are diverse according to their cause: some fractures are arched and others occur as stars; and some fractures depend on the length of the bone, such as fractures of the ulna and radius, and also there are fractures of the carpal bones, and also there are disruptions and fissures.
And each kind has its own special pain or discomfort during healing, according to the location of the fracture; and we will discuss these separately in their respective locations.
There are certain signs that indicate a bone fracture. And by these signs you can recognize a bone fracture: it is crooked and prominent; there is pain on pressure, and also when you press with your hand.
And therefore if there is no crookedness apparent in the bone, and the patient does not experience great pain when you press on it with your hand, then this is not a fracture.
There is a possibility that it is only a minor crack, and so you should not press on it with your hand, but rather apply medicines to the area; then wrap the area tightly with a bandage.
And you should know that when the bone is arched and not split in two or any other way that causes a break in the skin, then you may begin to treat it immediately with an cauterization of the wound; then wrap the area tightly with a bandage.
And if pus has accumulated in the wound, then let it drain for several days until the pus stops; and then treat it according to the nature of the wound and its pain.
And you should know that healing and hardening of the bone is a slower process than when it occurs in other bones.
And then strengthen it according to what we will discuss below.
But if the bone is split in two parts at the joint of the leg or hand, then it is necessary to extend that affected limb in two parts, with the help of the hand or foot, or with just one hand, or with a cord and the hand.
And hold the limb in its natural position, while you extend the two parts of the broken bone outward; then you will have to pull them back together as strongly as possible, and according to any method that can be used on these joints.
And be careful that your movements do not cause any pain or injury to the patient during this operation, and apply as much strength as you can, while holding one bone against the other in their normal position.
And you should apply as much force as possible with your hand while holding the bone, and if you find some other cause, then straighten it out and harden it according to the amount of strength that you have.
And avoid violent extension and compression, as this can cause pus to form in the wound due to their operation, or it can also cause contraction of the limb, as we have seen happen many times due to their operations.
And the degree of tightness of the bandage should be one that you can tolerate; for you should know that broken bones vary in their size and shape.
And for those that are small like the arm, hand and fingers, then a fine piece of linen is used to wrap them.
But those that are large like the thigh or leg, then a hard bandage is used to wrap them; for a tight bandage connects the large bone and strengthens it all around evenly, so that no swelling can enter.
And then you should begin by loosening the apparatus, stopping it from time to time in that place, over the fracture with three or four bandages according to the size of the bone; and strengthen your hand a little with the bandage.
And then bring him to the most superior part of the area of the fracture, and with great care so that you do not break your grip on the area of the fracture. Then lengthen and loosen the bandage around the area of the fracture a little, and ease the tightness little by little while you take hold of a large part of the area.
Then take another bandage and wind it around the other side of the fractured area, and then bring the bandages to the lowest part of the fracture; and your operation on the tightness of the bandage and its loosening should be according to what we have said about the first bandage above.
Then place between the bandages of wool or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is equalized if there is crookedness in it; and if not, then do not put anything at all in it.
Then place another bandage over it and tie those decently made layers together at once, if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and then place on it something that stops the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave it for several days, and then tighten the layers.
And the splints should be of the thickness of a straw or reed, and they should be cleverly formed; or they could be of wood or palm branches; and the length of each splint should be according to this form and figure:
However, the splint that is placed on the fracture should be thicker and longer than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how long the limb is in size and shortness; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightness; and then tie it above decently according to what we have said about tightness, and let your tightness be according to the location of the fracture more, and as much more as it is lengthened from the fracture, so much the lessness of the tightness.
And let the threads be moderate in thickness and thinness, and they should be of linen; and if the threads are thick, then as I have seen through the operation of some foolish people tying their threads with rope and it is a great error when the tightness is not equal.
And very thin threads are not convenient, because you will not achieve with them through tightness what you want, and let there be between each splint no less than a finger's width.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints when they are tightened in their places, then place below them wool or dyed wool so that it does not harm him.
