Chapter two: Of fractures when they occur in the head
The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct; for some fractures are caused by the impact of a heavy object, and the impact goes through the whole bone, breaking it as it goes, just like it does to a log, and for this reason that type of fracture is called a split fracture; or the heavy object hits only one part of the bone and breaks into the flesh but does not go through to the other side, and for this reason this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the size of the fracture is either large or small, and it may be narrow or broad; and the cause of it is impact from a stone or fall on a rock, and their likenesses.
And this fracture may be penetrating so that it approaches the membrane that is below the bone; or it may be in the face of the bone, and the size of these two fractures may be large or small.
And another type of fracture is that which occurs in the bone, in the thinness of the hair, and for this reason it is called a hairline fracture.
And there is another fracture that occurs from falling or being hit by a stone and its likenesses, when a thin layer of the bone is forced into the openings within it, and this occurs in concave places just like it does to the fingers when they are hit and their tips are forced inward.
And most often this happens to the bones of bent bodies, like the skulls of infants.
And all these types of fractures are set in such a way that they are separated from each other. And you will remember all this in its place.
And you will know all these types of fractures by detection of them and by inquiry into their likenesses, and by examination of the rotten flesh that is above them.
And hairline fractures are known by their appearance when the flesh is pulled back from the bone and washed clean; for then the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures you should first consider the signs of the patient, and then if you see any signs that clearly show fear, like vomiting from cholera, or extension, or loss of understanding, or loss of voice, or fainting, or acute fever, or sweating and heat in those parts, and likenesses to them from the major dispositions, then he will not heal.
But if you do not see these signs, then you should begin his cure.
The cause of this is that if the fracture has occurred at the beginning in the winter days, then you should study the healing of the bone over the course of fourteen days according to its entire disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then you should let the bone be bound for seven days, so that what is below the membrane can be corrupted by it, and so that the signs we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane and covered the brain, and it is narrow and sharp, then you should wet the sharp edge according to what I have told you.
And you should wash the head of the patient who is fractured, and uncover it according to custom and in a way that is possible for you according to the shape of the fracture, and so that it is loose on the patient.
And if you see the detection of the bone, or bleeding or an abscess, then you should stop it with what occurs, and so that humors flow from the wound into a cloth soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the fracture rest so that the abscess is relieved, and then begin the girding of the bone and its healing.
And this should be done according to one of two ways of operation.
And the first way is that you insert the bone with a narrow instrument, and this is its shape: And then you administer another instrument, one slightly broader than the first; and this is its shape:
And the other way is that you insert the bone through penetration with a narrow instrument, and this is its shape; and then you administer the broader instrument.
And you should have several instruments of different widths, some of which are broader than others, and some are narrower, and some are acute in the end, and made of iron or steel.
And they have ease of use in percussion on the scalp to prevent concussion and lead him to that.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then you must pierce through its surface before administering the scalpels, which are called non-deep scalpels.
And they are not named like this except that they do not reach the bone of the skull to what is right next to it, so that the scalpel has a rounded tip or edge below the sharp tip of it, which prohibits being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take many of these scalpels, each one according to the thickness of each bone, so that they are present in all skulls of the brain, whose end is sharp, and the amount in length and brevity is according to the thickness of this skull.
And this is the form of three species of scalpels: The quality of piercing through the broken bone is that you place the scalpel on the bone and turn it with your fingers so that you know that the bone has been penetrated; then change the scalpel to another place, and pause where there is a quantity of thickness of the vein or around it.
And then incision with scalpels what is between each crack of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can on top of it at the end of elevation, thus as we have said, so that you radiate the bone, or by your hand, or by any other cause by some instruments which you will have prepared for this, such as tweezers and subtle forceps.
And be sure to keep the incision away from any cause of the brain.
And then when you have pierced the bone and separated it from the brain if it is continued with it, and you will have extracted that, scrape and gnaw that in its roughness with another instrument similar to an incision, but however, let it be more thin and more subtle than the other scalpels.
And if you pull out some of the small bones and threads, then take that with ease from what is due to you from the instruments; then cure the wound with lint and bandages which we have said.
And the other manner of operation is lighter, distant from fear, which Galien and Lauzeck praised for great praise; and this is their speech about it: it is necessary for you in the beginning to begin to uncover the part of the bone of the place where there is the most violent and most manifest fracture, so that when you have uncovered that part and placed below it the tip of this scalpel slow and gentle, of which this is the form:
And the lenticular part is round and does not insert into any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides in length, as well as a wedge; let the lenticular part be applied to the brain with the sharp face of it inserted into the bone.
And then percute on the scalpel of one part with a small hammer so that you insert all the bone with ease as it is turned, and be sure of the membrane that it does not happen to him accidentally, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
Then if he removes any cause attached to the bone of the membrane in some places of bones, cut that from it with the tip of the lenticular scalpel, and free that from it with ease, because it is freed from him without harm and fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the membrane, and what is cut off or remains of the bone is the face of it, and pulls in it roughness and subtle threads, then scrape that roughness and gnaw those threads with subtle razors by which you have prepared their number, of different quantities so that it is possible for you to administer them in every place most conveniently, and according to what place you are performing your operation on that broken bone.
And be sure to administer in your reason with which you scrape the bone at the beginning of the most side of those razors, and then use that most subtle one, and do not cease to do that according to continuity until you come to administer the most thin and the most subtle of all of them.
But in any other small capillary fractures and subtle fracture, be sure to cure it according to what you come to rectify it.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And we have written about what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the membrane is extended when you radiate the bone, then take cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the hole of the wound; then take another cloth folded or doubled and soak it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth and do that as gently as you can so that you do not weigh down the brain; then administer over that bandage with fat length, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound has so much only.
And leave that with this cure for a day or two so that you are sure of cauterized pus; then loosen it and then administer medicines with which there is excessive dryness, such as linen rags and errhines of linseed, and powder of aniseeds and aristology, and of similar things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder which is sprinkled over the wound, as they are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which there is one that cleanses and does not bite.
And be careful according to your virtue that the wound in your cure is clean; and do not let there be any putrefaction or other in it; nor do you leave virulence to settle in it; because when it is applied over the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and rots it, and malevolent malice happens about the patient.
And it happens in the skull of the head, as when the bone is cleansed and most when the cure of it is diminished, and there is blackness on its surface; then consider therefore if it happens to the patient the accidents which we have said, know that it is mortal without doubt.
And if blackness does not happen except from the medicine which is placed over it, and it is because of that medicine that makes that blackness, then be sure to take some honey and parts of rose oil iii., and with those lines a cloth, and then place that over the skull; then cure it with the spices of cure which are convenient so that it is healed.
Chapter iii.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts very much uppermost, or one of those some few times, because in it there are two bones, because the part below is cartilage, and is not broken, and it does not happen to it except tension and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then be sure to insert your little finger in your nose, and gnaw that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your middle finger push it outward, so that the nose returns according to its natural figure. And do this for you with ease, and be sure that it does not hit because of your operation on the pain of them.
And if the fracture is in the highest part of the nose, and the patient does not perceive it, then one must make a small incision at the end of the bone where there is some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should act accordingly.
And provide treatment for him on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then return to it or wait seven days after the formation of the blister.
Then insert a piece of linen thread into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part; or put two splints in, if it is in two parts, and let there be as much thickness in the splint as will fill the crack in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorators say that the splints should be wrapped in wool, and changed every day, but this has not been seen by me; they say that the thread should be wrapped in alum-soaked linen, that the bone may be held firm, and the cartilage hardened.
And in some cases a feather quill is inserted into the nose instead of a splint; and this should be done when the fracture is very violent, and the patient not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause does not need you to act; and if you want to do it, then do it; and if you want to make splints, then make them.
And if another blister occurs, plaster the nose with cerate or cotton soaked in wine vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if a blister does not occur, then plaster that part with sesame flour and powdered onions cooked in alum vinegar; and place over it a piece of linen, and do not apply any other thing to the nose.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed, then cut them, and extract them with instruments appropriate to those; and close up the cut, and cure it with something that encourages flesh-growth and bone consolidation, and plaster it with convenient plasters.
And if pus occurs within the nose, then cure that with splints and administer lead tubes to ensure complete healing.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the mandible is broken and the fracture does not have pus, then consider whether the fracture is only external, and not broken in two and curved inward at its parts; then its treatment is easier.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left mandible, then insert the said index finger of the right hand; and move with that the dislocation inward at its parts with facility by means of the other hand at the part of the bone outward; decently equaling those.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then administer extension of both parts according to rightness so that its position is possible.
And if disruption of teeth occurs from concussion, then strengthen those which you hope will break with silk thread or silver wire or cedar; and plaster the broken mandible with cerate, and place over it doubled linen, and over that decently made great steel plate, or a sole weight at the length of the mandible; and tie that above according to how you are prepared for its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and quietness, and give him drink of sorbents of wine.
And if you judge that it has already been altered in some way, then you treat and loosen it on the third day; and correct what has been altered, and plaster it with alum vinegar, or with sesame flour, since you have removed its cerate, and place over it linen wool.
And after that, when the plaster is in place and adjusted over it, and the position of the bone is not altered, then leave it, so as not to loosen it until it is healed and the fracture strengthened; for many times this fracture is healed within three weeks.
And if another blister occurs, then administer what we have said several times in the treatment of it until it is healed, and let that blister be lanced.
And if the fracture has pus in it, then consider whether the bone is separated or shattered; then you act subtly that you remove those shatters with whatever is appropriate for their removal.
And if the opening of the pus is narrow, then you enlarge it with a flebotomy according to the quantity of your need.
And when you have removed those shatters, and if they do not ooze pus again, then close up the opening of the pus, if it is large; and if not, plaster over it one of the plasters appropriate for that, so that it encourages flesh-growth and is healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is dislocated.
Many times the clavicle in front of the breast is broken by the muscle; and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
For it is either broken, or separated, or separated in two parts other than those which do not occur shatters; and its treatment is easier; or occurs therein shatters, and it is difficult to treat; or the fracture has pus in it.
And its action in that, when the fracture is without pus, is that you have two agents, so that one holds the helper and follows the collarbone broken, and the other extends the collar to that part.
And then join the fracture with your fingers within it so that its form is made according to how it should be, and there is not a little prominence or concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then let the patient hold under his chin a pillow or some wool, and the size of it according to the quantity of your need; and extend and raise the collarbone and stop the holding with your hand within that you join the fracture according to how it should be.
And if you cannot draw the end of the collarbone to its parts outward, so that it is approached to the breast, then let the patient recline on his neck, and place over the muscle of it a pillow on the left in size.
And the agent press the muscle of it to the parts below so that the bone of the collarbone is raised that which is in the breast above; and then you correct the fracture and join it with your fingers.
But if you feel shatters of the collarbone and they are oozing, then let loose over that and extract those shatters with facility.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you invent an incision of it with one of the saws which you have prepared for that, since you had paused below the collarbone the tool which serves the ciphac; and this instrument is of wood or iron, and its form is such:
And it is similar to a chisel of which its concavity is such; and its breadth according to what you have need for it, according to the size of the bone or the weakness of it.
But its length is according to what is possible for you in the operation; that it have two ends thus reversed, and one end be ample and the other narrow.
If the wound, which you have opened when you extracted the splinters from the bone, is wide and you are sure that there is no abscess, then you stitch together the two lips of the wound.
And if the wound is small, or you fear an abscess, then you fill the wound with lint and powder according to the size of the wound.
And if there is an abscess, then pour wine, honey, and rose oil onto it and place a poultice over the area.
Also, make sure that the stake is not pushing against the broken bone; and therefore, when you see the patient every day, if you notice that the ligament has loosened or the stake appears to be pushing out, then rectify it and tighten the binding.
Then place the pillow under his neck and another pillow underneath his head, on which he should sleep at night; in such a way that by this means, the shoulder on that side is raised, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone is also elevated above the level of the muscle; or his arm on that side is pulled up towards his neck.
Do not loosen the binding unless there is pus or an abscess within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see that his face is changing due to it; and tighten the binding, and restore him until he is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and encouraged according to the month in twenty-eight days; and sometimes in some people, it is done within less time than that.
Chapter 6:
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The spatula is broken on its broad side and not on its edges.
And therefore when it is broken — and it is broken in its middle part — and it is not known except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture you prepare it and reduce it according to the natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
Then place onto the site powdered wheat flour and egg white, and lion's blood; then place a double-layered piece of linen; also place over that a wooden stake as thin as possible according to the width of the spatula or more.
But if under the stake is a hollow space in one of the places of the spatula, then fill that hollow with lion's blood and then raise the stake according to its equality.
Then tighten it above with long honey and decent constriction, and with the firmness of the stake so that it is not pushed out from its site.
And consider the binding every day; and whenever it becomes loose, make it tighter again and adjust the stake if it is pushed out from its site.
And keep the patient on his unaffected side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-fifth.
And therefore when this number is completed, then you dissolve the binding; and know that it is of the bones from which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it progresses from the bone's site and you place a weight under it, then you split it, and remove it, and proceed according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone regarding an abscess.
Chapter 7:
On the restoration of a leg fracture.
The leg sometimes is broken in its middle part and this is usually the case; but its ends are often not joined and comminuted.
And regarding its accidents when the middle is broken it inclines to the inner parts and there occurs great pain in it, and difficulty in walking, and coughing, and strong vomiting and bleeding; and a cavity is declared in the broken bone and it does not remain hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be laid on his back, and place a cushion between their shoulders; and press against their muscles, and adjust their ribs with the hands of two people, and gently in the operation and in the adjustment according to every way that is possible and convenient for it, so that the figure of the bone returns according to how it should be.
Then place onto it plaster and lint, and over that a thin stake of ash or of lime, or similar, after you have wrapped that in linen; also gently bend that over the broken bone so that it is not pushed out; and go around it with binding according to its roundness on the back sometimes, and tighten that with decent constriction; and consider the binding every hour, and when it becomes loose, tighten it.
And if it causes detachment due to swelling or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the site, then you cut and loosen it, and remove the plaster and adjust what has occurred due to its way of curing; and then return the binding, if there is a change in his face; and tighten him so that he is healed.
Chapter 8:
On the restoration of ribs when they are cracked.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in large sites that go along the back; and from their ends in front, it does not occur to them unless they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from feeling due to the inquiry with fingers.
And its restoration is that it should be joined with fingers according to the possible way so that it is swallowed according to how it should be; and then plaster and tighten the broken bone with a stake if it requires it.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines to the inner parts, then there occurs to the patient violent pain and piercing, also with piercing that occurs where it has pleaded, and because of this there is difficulty in walking, and coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is of difficult curing.
And the Aンティクス have been applied to it with very heated instruments.
And from them there is issued: that one should stop foods for the patient that cause inflammation and fullness, so that the belly is flattened and extended, and the fracture is driven to the outer parts.
But we abstain from this so that it is not the cause of gout, if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then let us help it.
And they will order some of them that it should be stopped over the wound and that it sucks with strength; and it is like reasoning, but it is feared that the wind draws away the superfluities to the site due to weakness.
And they said among themselves: whoever is in charge of the patient, let him be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil, and let there be powder placed between his ribs where it can be filled, so that the binding is as round as a ball; and let the patient be treated with poultices of food and medicine.
And if there is a violent constriction that the patient cannot endure, and the bones puncture the veil with their teeth, they fear for the patient; therefore, whoever incises over the affected area, exposes the broken rib, and places upon it an instrument that retains the phlegm, the memory of which precedes, and incises the bone with ease to extract it.
And then, sew up the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a stitch, and treat it with poultices until it is healed.
And if there is an abscess inside, then cut and pour powder in warm oil over the area; and let the patient be treated with something that treats the abscess inside.
And let him sleep on his back until he is healed.
Chapter 9:
On the restoration of a fracture of the spine and of the neck.
Sometimes there is a fracture in the bones of the neck, and they rub against each other with pain, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider the following: If their bones are already soft and stupid and dead and cannot move or bend any more, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and there is no pain, then know that they will not be healed, and in most cases it is lethal.
And if they are painful and there is a constriction when you press them or prick them, then know that the neck of the bone is saved and the patient will be healed with treatment.
And if you see something similar in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Now, if you see that they are already soft and there is something similar to what happened in the bones; and if when he lies on his back, he experiences windiness and involuntary defecation, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is lethal and you should not continue with his treatment.
But if none of these things happen, the prognosis is better and recovery is more likely.
And the treatment for him is to apply a poultice of hot oil to the abscess on the fractured vertebra with cauterized wool or with flowers of hound's tongue, and to apply it sometimes during the day as long as the abscess is suppurating.
And then apply one of the comforting and soothing poultices; and strengthen it with a bandage, and instruct the patient to take rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side that causes him pain until he is healed.
And if there is a separation of the bones due to some cause, then cut and dry the cord, and let them put it on the bone; and then sew up the two lips of the wound with a stitch if it is large, and treat it with something that encourages the growth of new flesh until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken and it is the end of the spine, then let him insert his left index finger into the anus, and hold together the broken bone with his right hand as much as possible due to the second condition; and then apply a poultice over it and splint it, if there are any signs of pain, and then bind it.
And if you feel that the bones are broken in him, then cut and dry over it, hold it, and treat the wound with what has been mentioned until it is healed.
Chapter 10:
On the restoration of a hernia.
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and when they are broken, their fracture is not only in their extremities but also in their length, and they decline towards each other; and there is pain and puncturing in the patient at that location, and the leg is stunned when it is fractured.
And its restoration is for you to extend your hand over it so that it lies over the fracture in the same way as its shape.
And if it is fractured at its end only, then hold it together according to what appears appropriate to you through an equal binding so that it is similar in shape to its natural form.
But if the fracture is in its length and it inclines towards the feet, then let the patient lie on his belly so that you can restore that fracture. And when you have bound it, apply a poultice over it, and then place over it a splint of wood or cord, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear the puncturing of the fracture or the removal of the splint.
And hold the cavity with something that fills it so that it takes on the binding according to its equality, and instruct the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if there is an abscess, then stop its extension and its restoration until the abscess is suppurating, and apply over it what has been mentioned before; and then return to its restoration, and bind it in the same way as previously described.
And if they are broken or crushed, then treat them.
Chapter 11:
On the restoration of a fracture of the elbow.
The elbow is what is between the shoulder and the head.
And if it is fractured, then its restoration is in one of two ways, and the first is that you take wood of a medium thickness according to this form:
And let it be tied at its two ends with two threads, and then let it be suspended from the raised location.
And let the patient lean on one of his hands; and then let him bend his broken arm over the wood so that it reaches the tip of the wood at the location where the wood is attached to the curve of the wood.
And then suspend it with some heavy weight, or let the servant extend it towards its sides; and then let the doctor and his assistants press the fracture with their hands, so that the fracture returns as it was before.
And the other way is for the patient to lie on his neck and suspend the arm of the collarbone with a bandage; and then let two servants hold one of them holding what is over the fracture with their hands, and the other the patient's feet, and each of them extend it towards its side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then bind over the fracture with a bandage and another one over that, and each of the two servants extend it towards its two sides.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then let half of the bandage be over the tip and the other under the fracture towards the shoulder.
And similarly, if it is close to the shoulder, then let the bandage be over that location and over the shoulder.
Hold it with ease and without pain, and then when it is bound according to its shape and there is no abscess, then bind it tightly if there is no abscess.
But if there is an abscess, then loosen it on the seventh day, and apply over it wool soaked in vinegar and warm oil so that the abscess is suppurating, and then bind it tightly.
And the manner of strengthening that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm onto that support, and place your open hand over his muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen the support for the arm so that the arm is in place of the broken bone, unless prohibited by any cause of the broken bone.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the others; and place between each splint the width of your finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an addition of three fingers of all together.
And then strengthen over the splints with that binding which has been said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is that your binding over the site of the fracture be more tight. And when the fracture is longer, let the binding be shorter.
And therefore if you see the placement of the splints and binding as we have said in the time when you restore the limb to its position, then do as follows:
And if you fear an ulcer, then you should leave off the binding and splints for seven days as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occur in the area; or it is prohibited that food come to the limb due to excess of binding; and therefore rectify all that according to what has been said.
But if you are certain that no such cause will occur, then do not loosen the binding but rather three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and let his hands be over his stomach.
And place over the support a poultice full of wool soaked in water, and consider throughout the night or day that the figure of the broken limb is not deflated nor dissolved, or that the binding be softened; therefore rectify all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have mentioned before, so that the food be subtle in the beginning as it is commenced strengthening; and then cover that he grows fat.
For it is customary for the support and his bed to be tightened for 40 days; and then cover that they be loosened and a bath be administered and he be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is white, then you should not loosen that binding and splints for 15 days or two months.
Chapter .12.
Of restoration of a fracture in the arm.
An arm is made up of two bones which are called two radii; one of which is small, and it is that which the thumb presses, and the other is large and is that which is pressed below by the small finger, in the part lower down.
And often only the larger radius is broken, or the smaller, and often they are both broken together.
And when the lesser radius is broken above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even easier.
And when it is broken below the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the lesser radius above, then it is convenient for the doctor, in order to facilitate its restoration, that he apply a small extension with ease.
But if the larger radius is broken, then cover that he apply more violent extension.
And if both radii are broken together, then cover that you apply motion most strongly.
And cover that you place the hand's shape for its restoration and extension stretched out over a pillow, and let the wrist of the hand be higher than all other parts, and let the thumb be highest of all the fingers, and let the patient be lying on his back with his arm stretched out over one cushion.
And there should be a pillow under him in the height or bend of him, so that the patient does not labor.
And then one assistant lengthens the arm from below with his hand or with binding, and another assistant lengthens above similarly.
And then the doctor soaks the bone in order to reduce it according to the best figure he can.
And if in the making of the bone there are bumps, then pierce through all hardness in its place with your skill.
And if an appearance of separated bumps shows up which press the tendon and it is not in your hope for its restoration, then you should pierce through it and correct that according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with swelling, then I have already made a particular chapter about that, and therefore take its cure.
And if an ulcer occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be in between being thin and soft, and strengthen the cloth over that with subtle binding so that the ulcer yields; and then remove the cerate and apply plaster prepared from powdered mulberry pulp, and then place splints; and let the splint which is placed over that fracture be wider and stronger.
And know that the number of the arm's splints you will see in the major dispositions, or let the fracture be in one radius or in both radii together.
And then strengthen your binding over the site of the fracture most strongly and most violently, and when you loosen the binding on top of the fracture or at the bottom parts of it, loosen the binding a little softer according to how it is preceded in the beginning of this chapter.
And the clothes which are wrapped over the fracture should be soft and moistened with oil, and not hard nor rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be between thin and thick, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the binding for a few days. And if any cause occurs which necessitates correction, as we have mentioned earlier when it occurred to the limb, then cover that you embrace the limb with warm water so that that yielding ceases.
And let the limb not be restrained while it rests, and then tighten the binding.
And if the binding is already softened and the bone is bathed and like those, then rectify all with your skill.
And consider also if food is prohibited from coming to the limb due to excess of binding, then cover that you soften it a little and let it be for a few days so that the food comes to it, and then strengthen it.
But if in the patient there occurs none of those causes which we have said, then do not cover that it be loosened for 20 days or thereabouts from those.
And then let the patient's hand be suspended by its virtue of active movement, and let him sleep on his back.

Chapter .13.

Chapter .xiv.
On the restoration of a broken thigh bone.

The binding of it is done thus: take a hard bandage and fold it two or three times over the break, and remove the superfluous length from it; then wrap the leg so that it comes to the ankle and the root of the thigh.

Then leave the binding over it as long as there is no corruption or abscess or inflammation and likewise to those; and if any of these occur, then treat them with removal of the binding and correct what has happened in each case, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a wound on the bone, then you should heal it according to what is possible for you; and if not, leave it and cure with that of which the account has been preceded so that it is healed.
And sometimes this break of the thigh is bound with splints in addition to those we have said for the thigh and arm.

Chapter .xvi.

Chapter .xix.
Of the restoration of the bones when they are with plague.

Let him be laid down flat on his back, and let there be no awkwardness in his posture so that he is not bent over during the operation.

So that the lower end of the bone should be elevated above the upper end of the prominent bone.

And then give a poultice at the end of your treatment of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can apply the traction with forceps and the use of bandages according to the amount of the wound.

And then heal the wound if you see it, and if not, leave it.
Chapter .XX.
On the cure of nodules that happen in the path of a fracture.
Many times this nodule happens in the path of healing a fracture, and usually it is near the joints, and it strongly prohibits the limb from its natural action.
Therefore consider if the nodule is recent and apply to it medicines which have tightened like aloes, and onions, and mallow, and thistle, and similar things; in such a way that you take some or all of them, and mix them with wine-based medicines, or with white vinegar, or with sour wine, and place that on the nodule with a sponge, and bind it tightly.
And let the ligature not be loosened for many days; and then loosen it and repeat another, in such a way that the nodule is rubbed; or bind tightly over that a sheet of lead descentment, for lead has the property of rubbing everything which is swollen in the limbs.
But if the nodule is already turned to stone and it is tight and it causes necessity to cut that with a saw, then cut it at the part which is most prominent from it, and insert the superfluous and prominent, or remove it with some saws in such a way that it is rubbed; and heal that until it is healed.
Chapter .XXI.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then what happens to the limb which is sottile and frail not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the limb becomes sottile and frail, this happens for many causes: of which one is abundance of solution of the ligature and of its binding not according to what is appropriate; or by superfluity of stricture of the ligaments in such a way that the nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or by abundance of superfluous swelling; or by superfluous movement not at the right time; or by deficiency of blood in the body of the patient and of his weakness.
And the cure for this is to give food to the patient and make his heart tender in such a way that blood is multiplied in him; and apply poultices; and place leeches and let them remain on it.
And then place a bandage on the limb, in such a way that the bandage draws nourishment to it; or continue to rub it with warm water in such a way that the nourishment flows and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .XXII.
On the cure of bones which are broken and then restored crookedly, and prohibited from their action by what is appropriate.
When it happens to the limb already restored that its healing of crookedness and prominence of the broken bone and nodule, and is thus weakened in form, but however the limb is not prohibited from its natural action, then do not accept the word of him who examines that limb as if it were newly broken.
But already there are many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is most reproachable, presenting the sign of which is mortal.
If the limping and nodding are recent, then they should be treated with a compress of warm water in which soft herbs are steeped, such as leaves of alder and its roots, and sweet-smelling plants similar to these; or he should be given an ointment made from a softening ointment base, such as one made from liquid paraffin; or a poultice of alder root gum should be applied, either with cotton wool or with olive oil, and then bandaged with that; or fat figs should be cut into pieces and bound with pigeon feathers, and similar remedies to those mentioned for strengthening the joints.
And sometimes it is dissolved by frequent gentle friction with moist hands, and the limb should be moved in all directions at all times.
But if the twisting has gone too far and become strong and turned to stone, and requires treatment with iron, then the pus should be drawn out from above and the continuity of the bone should be dissolved, and that which is superfluous of the nodule of that joint should be cut away with thin instruments, and ease should be administered to it through solicitude and study.
Chapter 23.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its place, by which it is prohibited from moving, and the shape of the limb is distorted, and it brings great pains and serious injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when dislocation occurs, it should be reduced immediately, for if it is delayed, the joint will become inflamed and very strong, and the reduction of the dislocation will then be difficult.
And for that reason, he should not be beaten nor stretched in the hour of inflammation, for many times it causes spasms and pains to the patient.
But when it happens, you should apply the flaubotomy to the patient, and let him rest so that the inflammation may subside; then the limb should be rubbed with warm water and oil, and reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated according to what its memory will bring it back to its place.
And I have arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what precedes in fractures, from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
Of the cure for dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are dislocated if not chewed; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are beaten out of their joints by a little movement, and become soft a little; or they are completely dislocated and filled, so that they are softened at the joint where saliva runs from the patient's mouth, and he cannot hold it closed nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his speech.
But when the dislocation of him is slight, then in the most dispositions it returns by itself for no cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and filled, then the reduction of it should be administered gently, and not be delayed at all.
And the minister should hold the patient's head, and the doctor should put his finger of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the patient' s mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or he should put both fingers in together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands be outside holding his cheeks; and he commands the patient to stop his jaws and open them in every direction; and the doctor should hold the jaw and push it back into its place.
And if the reduction of him is difficult, and especially if both jaws are both dislocated, then administer evaporation with warm water and oil, so that the reduction of both be made gently and not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore when they are returned and closed, and the mouth of the patient is not softened, then apply powders of cloth with cerate, and let it be made of wax and oil rozat; and then tie it with ease by means of mild ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that he may not beat it to the right nor to the left; and do not force him to chew for any cause; having his food be lean lion meat, so that when the pains are beating and the jaws are numbed, then let him beat what he will.
And that should be administered with ease, and do not labor over the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and yawning, so that the jaw may be numbed and healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any hour and not returned to their places, then many times it happens that fevers and constant sweating accde, and very strong colic is provoked in the belly of the patient, and very strong vomiting of pure colic.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times that person dies to whom it happens in six days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collar bone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collar bone is not dislocated within by its continuity with the chest; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer end, and that is declared by feeling.

And then apply ointment and powders and strengthen that.

Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the tip of the finger at the bottom part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the top of the muscle, and that is done quickly; and it is not dislocated at the sides behind the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and drawn out at the bottom parts behind the finger, and mostly in those of which the flesh is few, it is dislocated quickly; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is drawn out with difficulty.

Chapter .XXVII.
Of cure of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and most of all to the front part than behind, and its dislocation to you is not hidden, for when you adjust the neck dislocated in any way, it is declared to you manifestly, for the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm, nor touch with it the muscle.
And let you know that if it comes to the patient with an abscess cautery, then its reduction is difficult, and especially when it comes to it with an abscess cautery, for it is not healed at all; and most of all when it comes to the back part, for worse than all other species of dislocation, and more pain, and many times they die from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of the patient be extended with two hands, and the arm of the patient be expanded, and the hands of the doctor be on the neck and under it, and he push the joint with his thumbs of his hands inwards, or with the hollow of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front part, then it returns in this way that he bends the hand with his own hand, while he pierces the neck with the hollow of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer violent extension and very hard.
And let two ministers extend the arm, and hold it tightly so that it is not pulled back from the extension.
And then let the arm be rolled all around, since it is wrapped in a long folded drape or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that it be helped by all causes which help in lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with violent pressure until it returns.
And then the reduction of it is that it be placed on the ointment in which it is stiffened and dried with honey, and let it be bound tightly with a bandage and the arm suspended by the neck, and let him be released for several days.
And then it is loosened; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the ligament of it and leave it there.
And if you see the joint not to be stronger, then repeat the plaster and the ligament, and leave it there for several days until it is strengthened; and then loosen it.
But if you see the patient become worse after the reduction of the joint, and the condition is in movement, then administer moistening baths and subtle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place a poultice on the joint, which is a mixture of grease; and then bind it, and leave it on overnight. And then remove the poultice, and place the patient in a bath; and when he is bathed, then rub the joint with tempered friction, and then repeat on that poultice, which is rosemary of oil, once, and the second time, and the third, corruption has already occurred in the brain through the instrument of bath water, so that it becomes thin.
And if you want, place on that wound of the cow's udder wet and roasted with butter and tightened over it; and do this several times, because that is wet and reduced to its condition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the elbow, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
Elbow of the hand is sometimes displaced, and the reduction of its dislocation is easier, and in contrast to the other joints, however, there must be swiftness in its reduction in the hour when it is displaced, lest the joint become pustular or pus occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's elbow on a table and extend his hand, and the doctor places his hand or palm on the protuberance of the joint, and push it in until it returns to its place.
However, there must be consideration if the palm is already placed on the stomach of the hand, then the patient places his forearm on a table opposite to extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is prior to the forearm, then let the position of the hand of that be intrinically on the table, so that the doctor's hand lies on that protuberance of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then tighten that with plaster, yielding the pustule and leave it; and do not repeat this, because unless more than days pass over it.
However, the joint bends according to its tortuousness; and does not feel any cause in the patient, except that the hand is softened and cannot strengthen anything; then you know that the nerves are broken or cut, and therefore it does not have strength in it except when it is tightened with cautery near its joint and that is strongly applied.
Therefore when the elbow is returned, then place on it plaster which we have recommended, and let it be left and tightened for several days; and then loosen it and let it be left and the hand of him exercised.
But if the movement of it is hindered and there is any heaviness in him, then loosen that with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter .XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are displaced entirely apart.
And when any of those fingers is displaced on the back of the palm or on its belly, then you press the finger and drive the dislocation of it with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that over the part to which it is displaced, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until it is straightened into that place on that day.
And when the morning comes, loosen that according to that relation, and do not cease loosening that during the day and exercising it with movement, and loosen it at night; and do this for several days until it is tightened.
And do the same with that if it is displaced on the belly of the hand, and loosen that to that place and do with it your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with that when it is displaced in other places.
Chapter .XXX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When it happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck and several vertebrae remain out of place, then there is no cure in that, because death approaches the patient.
And the sign of this is that the excretion of the patient is outside his will, and he cannot retain it; and sometimes some of his members are softened or two of his legs or arms, or one of those; or one vertebra is displaced from its place, because sometimes it is displaced and sometimes the removal of those to four places.
Therefore that is already displaced to the back parts and is called hunchback.
However the cure of that is that you consider if the hunchback happens from childhood, because then there is no cure for that and there is no healing of that at all.
But in that which comes from accident or from stroke or similarly to those, the Ancients have spoken of ways of cure with prolixous words, of which the most returned with profit; but I and you have abbreviated that of which the little excused of its excess, by my proximity to intention my exposition, which I have expounded and formed from diverse instrument of that, by which I show that and spread out that.
And of those are not cured except that which happens in the back properly with that which I have mentioned.
And let the patient be extended on his face over a pail, and be extended below him instrument moist so that his feet are not bound.
And then a straight post is placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the pail around the head of him, and another post in the air holding his feet at the other end of the pail.
And the minister held the posts, and are not closed in a hole; and another minister holds the other according to that form.
And then wrap around the head of the patient and hold it with a lion-skin strap; and extend the end of the strap to the post which is over the head of him, and loosen that in him; and then tighten the other strap on the loins of him, his knees holding his feet, and then adjust all the straps and loosen those in the other post which holds his feet.
And then the minister extends the strap to the end of the post, and let the two posts not be displaced and the straps of their places in which they are placed, but however do not close them as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the vertebra with strength until it returns, or he lies on a table, and then let him adjust his feet on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return by that form, then take a table of which the breadth or length is in air for three arms, and hollow in the wall of which we have said it is close to the patient a place in which the end of the table. And then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and stretch well until the spine is cramped and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a turn which is wound around the hand; and it is that you place on the ground under the patient's head in the back of the knee two fists, the length of which is a arm, and under his head in the back of the heel also two fists; and let the length between each fist be a palm, and already in each fist there is made a pull in which the turn runs, and each is very firmly fixed to the ground so that it is not moved.
And there is placed a round post in which the turn is wound, and it is wound around the two holes of the two fists.
And in its end there is a hole in which the post is made, the length of which is a palm where it is wound; and in the other two posts is similar to that.
And then stretch the ligaments which you have tightened in the foot of the patient in the hole which is under his head, and the ligaments which you have tightened in his thighs in the turn which is under his feet.
And then place each turn and have a minister twist the turn with his hand, and the doctor bends the spine as we have said.
And then when the spine is returned and the place is smoothed, then apply a paste of dried white lead and linen; and then place over the paste a wooden board of which the breadth is three digits or around that, and its length is the quantity that it takes up the space of the hernia, and over the part of the spine.
And then let it be bound with a ligament as we have said, and use subtle food for the patient until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the place at the end of healing, then apply medicines that melt and smooth with administration of the table for a long time, which we have counted; and already is administered in that place a plate of lead.
And sometimes an imminence happens in the back of the spine bones, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already imminent for which it is not possible to be made protrusion to him with this medicine, because quickly he will cause death to you.
Chapter .xxxi.
Of cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not cause but dislocation only, and do not happen to those which happen to other joints of small removal and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four ways, because it is dislocated at the parties inside, or at the parties outside, or at the parties in front, or at the parties behind.
And mostly it is dislocated at the parties inside, and seldom at the parties in front or at the parties behind.
And the sign of its dislocation at the parties inside is that when you bend the leg of the patient with the patient, it is longer and the thigh is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot because of the language, and the location of the language is pustulated by pustule cauterized, so that the head of the hip joint has come here.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at the parties outside is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at the parties in front is that they expand their leg according to its compliment, but indeed they cannot bend it without pain that is in the thigh; and if they insist on walking and not being able to walk over those on the front; and their urine is retained and their groin is pustulated, and due to their walking on their heel.
And the sign of that which happens to dislocation at the parties behind is that they do not expand the thigh, nor can they bend that in front that bends the groin, and their leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip joint is due to that place.
But reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and antique, which has already been ancient by having you administered reduction to it and remained according to its disposition, then there will not be complete cure in that; and therefore it is not possible for protrusion to happen to him.
But in that which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you are led to that which you want to turn the joint and extend it to the parties inside and to the parties outside, and from there to the right and left, because quickly it returns and does not need other cure.
But if it does not return in this way, then you prepare a strong minister and extend his leg from the lowest party, or with his own hands, or with ligament which is bound over the leg on top of the thigh; and another minister extends his hand over that below the nipples of him; and then stretch with ligament lion over the root of the hip, and hold the other minister the extremity of the ligament.
And its extension is from the inside party to the groin at the party of the collar bone, or from the back party to the party of the breast.
And let the extension of all those in one go as the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and that he hangs bent.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is well; and otherwise, it is necessary in every species that I will tell of their own cure.
In its own reduction, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is at the inside parties, let the patient be raised on his side; and then place the ligaments over the root of the hip in that place between the head of the hip joint and the party which is below the groin; and then extend the ligament from above the part of the groin to the highest party of the collar bone.
And then another strong minister takes his arms, and presses the thick part of the hip joint and extends to the outside parties by violent extension, because that returns to its place.
And this species is the quickest cure with which that member is reduced more than other species.
But if he is excused to you and does not obey the treatment with this species of cure in everything, then let his two feet be bound together with strong ligament, quickly, over both heels and over both thighs.
And let the length of each of its parts be the quantity of four digits, and let the hip joint be extended but that the other by a quantity of two digits.
And then let the patient be suspended over the head of the post, and that he is in the middle; and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two yards.
And then command a strong young man that lessens the head of the hip joint, and let him be suspended by another young man, and the other young man lessening the hip with strength pushes, because the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in its own reduction, when it is dislocated at the outside party, let him lie over the hole according as we have recommended while having hibernated; and let the ligament be tightened over the hip joint properly and over the feet of him.
And then two sticks are placed, one at the feet and the other at the head; and then sticks adjusted to the waist, with a damp cloth wrapped over the area, so that the patient is not harmed by the stick between their thighs, so that when the extension of the legs is drawn down, it is not pulled.
And then each assistant extends to their side, and the doctor has his hand ready to make the incision according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in front of the legs, then let the chest be slightly extended and it is according to this narrative on the waist; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the armpit of the patient, and tightly presses that with his other hand, and with that grip he extends the leg at the beginning of the groin.
And if the dislocation is in back of the legs, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended at the front of the legs and raised from the ground; but let them be placed on a hard surface, just as the positioning when the arm is dislocated outward, as we have said regarding his lying down on two arms; and he should be on his side, and the joints extended, as we have said, with no difficulty.
And there should be a compression applied with a board over the location where there is pain in the joint.
And when the reduction is complete according to what you have seen — and if the sign of the reduction is hidden from you, and it is that you extend both legs for the patient, and when you see these signs and the patient stretches their leg and lengthens it without excuse, then you should know that the limb according to what has happened is returned — and then you adjust two thighs and apply ointment and tighten with a bandage so that the joint is not damaged in any of its parts; and the patient must remain at rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the ligament and the bandage, and compare one leg to the other.
Then, if you see these signs in reality, then know that the dislocation is already closed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that, and tighten that according to the first extension, and let it be for three days.
And then loosen that, and let that delay in walking over anyone for several days, until he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder is dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated outward, and inward, and downward but not forward; and backward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to lift their arm with their shoulder, and then you know that it is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its kinds is that you force the patient to straighten their arm, as much as possible; adhere two fingers to their face, and place their foot between your feet; and then have another assistant extend their leg while the shoulder returns to its place.
And the sign of its restoration is that their arm adheres to their shoulder without laboriousness.
And then plaster that and have the arm adhere to the shoulder, and then tie those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then loosen them.
And do not use the patient of walking for several days until it is healed and comforted.
But if its reduction to you is excused, then administer strong extension by the ligaments of which in the cure of the hip there is preceding narrative.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure for the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by slight displacement and sometimes it is dislocated according to full, and its dislocation is done either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure for its displacement is slight reduction and is that it be extended with ease by hands and be covered while it returns.
But in its cure when it is dislocated according to full, you must force the patient straight, and have a strong and vigorous minister from behind his leg in the middle part of him.
And then you with your right hand hold the highest part of the foot, and with your left hand the lowest part of the foot in the location of the ankle.
And then draw the foot to you with your right hand, and then with your left twice, and then extend it with the left; and press the instep of the foot with the right because of the bandage of his labor.
And do that two times according to what we have counted.
And then press the instep of the foot to the ankle on the third occasion, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore if it returns in one or two according to this way, and if you see the foot swollen, it is good; but if not, then repeat the operation on it, because it will return.
But if you are prohibited from reducing it with what we have recommended, then let the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tighten a staff with a cloth in the ground, and that it lies between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around him so that he does not sweat.
And then the minister of it has his thighs, and another minister extends the foot by their hands or by the ligament that lies on the neck of the foot; and each minister extends against the extension of his fellow, and the staff is extended between the thighs of the patient, so that the body of him is not drawn to the front because of its extension.
And then the doctor holds the dislocation with their hands, and another minister has the healthy thigh at the front, because the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and you are told of its restoration, then apply plaster and tighten that with strong ligaments, and tie the foot to the ankle.
And you must fear or guard against the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the ligament on it is not violent and lead that strongly.
And then let him be for two or three days; and if the ligament is softened, tighten that.
And then loosen it on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that space, there is no guarantee that the dislocation is not dissolved and ruined, and then it receives no cure.
And if pustules occur to him in that place, then you must minister to the swelling of it where our narrative precedes, in another location of cure and fomentation while it is swollen.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
A cure for dislocated toes of the feet.
First, let the one who is dislocated be anointed with a little oil; and what is not difficult, let it be lighter.
But if the dislocation is in any part of the toes of the foot, then let him have his foot cut off from under him on the ground or on a table, and let him stand like that.
Then you stand on your feet and put your foot over that which protrudes from the joints, and then press it with your foot with force so that it goes back in, and see if it has been anointed and there is no protuberance in the joint.
Then put under his foot a table that covers the whole foot, which has two ends, and then tighten him with decent and firm tension for three days; and then loosen him and let him rest from walking for several days, until he is healed and his return is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the kinds of dislocation that are caused by a blow or by a fracture or by any other impingement.
When one of those causes occurs and you penetrate into the cure of that and into the restoration of him, many times death happens.
And because of that, you should not presume on the cure of him, but rather he who is wise in the art, with great compassion and pity, gently, not hastily or presumptuously, and who uses in the beginning of medicines which reduce swellings only so much, and let the patient be left to divine judgment, unless you hope for salvation from ruin, with alleviation of the illness, and in which there appears some hope. And then strive to reduce him immediately at the beginning of the cause, before he suffers an impingement.
And then if the member returns as we want, then administer treatment which reduces the swelling and heals the wound with that which is appropriate for him of the dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and it happens that the bones are separated, then you penetrate their joint, and do in that what we have said in the simple illnesses of those of which we have spoken earlier.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that instrument in the ways of fear according to what has preceded my preaching; for he who is best to you owes you your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and healthier to your true self those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasim perfectly, of which God be blessed. Amen.
